SUPRAMOLECULAR BODIPY DYE ARCHITECTURES FOR ADVANCED LIGHTHARVESTING AND ENERGY TRANSFER APPLICATIONS

ii

ABSTRACT SUPRAMOLECULAR BODIPY DYE ARCHITECTURES FOR ADVANCED LIGHTHARVESTING AND ENERGY TRANSFER APPLICATIONS Muhammad Yousaf Doctor of Philosophy, Molecular Science, Ryerson University, 2016

Shape-persistent phenylacetylene macrocycles have been explored in a number of optoelectronic and light-harvesting applications, including two-photon absorption. Likewise, BODIPY (4,4-difluoro-4-bora-3a,4a-diaza-s-indacenes) dyes have also been extensively used in material applications, owing to their tunable, intense absorption and sharp emission spectra exhibiting high quantum yields. Combining these two architectures, this thesis presents a derivative where a BODIPY is contained orthogonally within the phenylacetylene-macrocycle. This structure was inspired by photosystem II antennae complexes where energy is obsorbed by antennae complexes and funneled down to a reaction centre. Specifically, this family of molecules uses the phenylacetylene macrocycle to harvest the light and transfer it to the BODIPY core, and this has been observed by examining the fluorescence emission from the BODIPY (Figure 1). This novel dye design if coupled to a donor (D) and acceptor (A) capable of anchoring to TiO2 could be modified to become a -spacer (i.e. D--A motif) for dye-sensitized solar cells resulting in panchromatic absorption, and ultimately more efficient devices.

Chapter 1: Introduction
1.1. Solar Energy and the Environment
Humanity's insatiable demand for energy (13 terawatts (TW) in 2006) will likely increase to 30 TW by 2050. Currently, 85% of our global energy is generated from the combustion of fossil fuels, and this has led to the depletion of available reserves (needed for other applications) and has led to significant adverse effects on the environment. In order to address this issue, humanity must mitigate its consumption of fossil fuels with non-carbonaceous energy sources, and this represents one of the greatest technological (and social) challenges of our time.1,2 Of the major non-carbonaceous energy forms (wind, hydroelectric, biomass, geothermal, tidal, nuclear and solar), photovoltaic energy is the most likely source to meet our energy demands. Solar energy has a low environmental impact and gifts the earth surface with 183,000 TW of energy.3,4 Unfortunately, the problem with photovoltaic technology is that it currently is more expensive than the conventional sources, and as such, modern solar cells must be improved upon. Photovoltaic technology can be broadly divided into three generations. The first two generations of solar cells are based on either single-crystal silicon or thin-film (amorphous silicon (a-Si), Cd-Te, Ga-As, GaInAs, etc) technologies. The first two generations are highly efficient and robust owing to their inorganic nature, but their applications are limited due to either cost and/or the use of hazardous materials in these technologies. In order to replace traditional energy sources and expand our solar market to the TW scale, a substantial change in technology is required. To this end, third generation photovoltaic materials are being investigated to complement existing technologies. One of the most efficient next/third generation photovoltaic cells is the dyesensitized solar cell (DSSC).5 DSSCs have drawn considerable attention due to their interesting and promising conversion efficiencies (exceeding 13%).6-9

1.2.

Dye-Sensitized Solar Cells (DSSCs)
The original dye-sensitized concept was first reported in the late 1960s when Gerischer and

Tributsch worked on the sensitization of an organic dye (perylene) with an n-type zinc oxide semiconductor; however, the perylene showed poor power conversion.10,11 A significant
1

breakthrough was achieved when Grätzel and co-workers reported a DSSC (1991) with an efficiency of 7.1%.5 Since this time, DSSCs have improved considerably and are considered as promising alternatives for photovoltaic conversion of solar energy. In a DSSC, an electrically conducting glass substrate is coated with a thin (10-25 m) porous layer of titanium dioxide (TiO2) nanoparticles to create a modified anode. This anode is then further modified by adsorbing a dye molecule to the titania surface (Figure 1.1a). A platinized glass cathode seals the cell, and an electrolyte solution is added completing the cell fabrication (Figure 1.1b).

Figure 1.1. a) Construction of a DSSC device, and b) a representative optically transparent cell made in our labs; c) operational model of a Grätzel's cell (DSSC) with the arrows showing the movement of electrons: 1) light absorption and electron excitation from HOMO to LUMO of dye. 2) Electron injection from S* into the TiO2 semiconductor. 3) Electron percolates through the TiO2 and external load where it ultimately reduces the electrolyte. 4) Regeneration of dye by electrolyte

From an operational point of view (Figure 1.1c), when a photon is absorbed by the dye molecule (1), an electron is excited from HOMO (S) to the LUMO (S*). The excited electron has the thermodynamic driving force to be injected into conduction band of TiO2 (2). The electron percolates through the anode (3) and external load where it then reduces the redox-active electrolyte. The electrolyte then regenerates the dye (4) allowing the cycle to continue. As with
2

any voltaic cell, there are four main components that affect the performance of the DSSCs: the photosensitizing dye, anode, cathode and electrolyte.12,13 The dye is generally considered the powerhouse of the cell and the most likely source of cell deterioration and as such will be the major focus with this thesis and its attempt to make a more efficient DSSC dye.

1.2.1. Efficiency of DSSC The efficiency () of a solar cell, which gives the overall conversion of sunlight to electrical energy, is given by the short circuit current (Jsc), open circuit voltage (Voc), the fill factor (FF), and the intensity of the incident light (Pin) as according to the following equation.


where the fill factor is the ratio of the maximum power of the cell per unit area and Pin is the power of the incident radiation at AM (air mass) 1.5 (typically 1000 W/m2). To a first approximation, the VOC is related to the energy difference between conduction band of the semiconductor and electrolyte redox couple. Conversely, the JSC is related to the efficacy of the dye injecting electrons into the semiconductor. The JSC depends on the relative energy levels of the sensitizers and the kinetics of the electron-transfer processes at electrolyte/dye/semiconductor interface. During operation, certain undesirable things may happen. For example, the electrons from excited state (S*) can either decay back to the ground state or the electrons after being injected to TiO2, can go to the electrolyte or back to the dye directly without passing through the external circuit and reduce its performance. This short circuiting process is called recombination.

1.3.

Anatomy of DSSC dyes
With dyes being a key component in the DSSC architecture, the past two decades has

observed a tremendous variation in structure-property investigation when considering the electronic and optical properties of molecular dyes. To this end, two major classes of DSSC dyes have emerged; namely, transition metal-based dyes and organic dyes.

3

Owing to the original success of the Gratzel cell, ruthenium polypyridyl complexes such as the Black dye and N719 (Figure 1.2) received considerable attention and have been pushed to conversion efficiencies () >11%.14-16 However, the poor absorption beyond 600 nm, cost and low natural abundance of ruthenium,17 has shifted the paradigm towards organic-based dyes like Sun's dye that achieved more modest conversion efficiencies of () >7%.18 Organic dyes are advantageous because they offer a large diversity in the molecular structure and can be synthesized using a diverse library of reactions. It is also possible to more readily tune the absorption, emission and electrochemical properties through suitable molecular design strategies.19

Figure 1.2. a) Black dye b) N719 c) Sun's dye

In general, all dyes can be distilled to a requisite structure that contains a redox-active donor (D) that is coupled through a conjugated linker () to an acceptor (A) capable of anchoring to TiO218-22 (D--A molecular structure, Figure 1.3).

4

Figure 1.3. Organic dye motif for DSSCs

1.3.1. Redox active donors The donor part of the dye molecule should be robustly redox-active and should be able to effectively inject an electron into TiO2. In metal based dyes, this is typically the role of the redoxactive metal and a metal-to-ligand charge transfer driven process. However, in organic based dyes, the triphenylamine (TPA) group has been found to be a promising redox active donor for photovoltaic materials owing to its excellent electron-donating ability, high hole-transporting properties and stability. The TPA moiety can also be readily functionalized/tuned and the propeller-shaped molecular structure of TPA also suppresses the recombination of injected electron from TiO2 to the redox couple. As such, TPA and its derivatives have been extensively used in metal-free organic dyes for DSSCs.23-25

1.3.2. -spacers The donor and acceptor parts of the dye molecule are connected through -spacers. These spacers are significant as they increase the photocross-section of dye and charge separation in the excited state. In transition metal based dyes, the polypyridyl ligands typically play the role of spacer, but in organic systems thiophenes have been extensively employed.
5

The -spacer also dramatically influences the wavelength of absorption. To a first approximation, dyes obey the 1D particle-in-a-box model, and therefore extension of the box (conjugated system) leads to a decrease in the HOMO-LUMO gap (-*; Figure 1.4). More generally, however, adjusting the length of the conjugated spacer tunes the energy of the frontier molecular orbitals (FMOs) and concomitantly shifts the absorption profile for the desired application.26

Figure 1.4. Decrease in energy gap by increasing conjugation length

Thiophenes as the -conjugated unit are desirable owing to their chemical and environmental stability as well as their electronic tunability.18,27,28 However, the challenge of using thiophenes as -spacers is that with increased numbers of thiophene units there is a stronger propensity for intermolecular - interactions leading to dye aggregation ultimately decreasing the efficiency of organic dyes.23,29 Dye aggregation should be minimized in order to obtain maximum performance and this could be done by appropriate structural modifications.30,31 Substituting different alkyl groups on the -conjugated oligothiophene units23,32 show inhibited dye aggregation and improved efficiency.

1.3.3. Acceptors/anchor For an efficient DSSC, the dye must bind strongly to the surface of the TiO2 semiconductor and covalent bonding interactions provide the strongest linkage between the dye and the TiO 2.33
6

Although silane (SiX3 or SiOX3), sulfonate (SO3) and phosphonic acids have been used as anchoring groups, carboxylic acids have become the standard anchoring group owing to their ability to effectively bind to the semiconductor with relative stability and ease of synthesis.34,35 Furthermore, carboxylic acid derivatives (i.e., ester, acetic anhydride, acid chloride, or amide) have also been used. Cyanoacetic acid is the most common organic acceptor because of its strong electron-withdrawing effect within the dye motif that improves charge separation in organic systems. Carboxylic acid groups can bind to the TiO2 surface via different binding modes as shown in figure 1.5.36,37

Figure 1.5. Binding modes for carboxylate unit on TiO2 surface

1.3.4. Designing effective organic dyes While designing dyes for DSSC applications, it is important to first understand how molecules absorb light. Dyes should be deliberately designed to absorb strongly in the visible and near IR regions of the electromagnetic spectrum, because that is where the greatest photon flux resides in the solar spectrum. Furthermore, in order to favor electron injection into the semiconductor upon photon absorption, significant charge separation must occur between the ground and excited state (Figure 1.6 a & b). In organic metal-free dyes, the charge transfer sensitization is driven by a -to-* transition. As shown in Figure 1.6, when a photon is absorbed, the electron density in the HOMO, located principally on the redox-active donor portion (TPA), is excited towards the * orbital of the dye (i.e., cyanoacetic acid) through a -spacer (thiophene). The electron is then injected from the * orbital into the semiconductor (Figure 1.6c).

7

Figure 1.6. Electronic redistribution between HOMO and LUMO (a, b), leading to charge

injection into the semiconductor (c) upon photoexcitation of Sun's Dye18 In order for charge transfer to take place, a molecule's propensity to absorb light in the UV-Vis-NIR is a significant quantity. When designing a dye with an intense absorption envelope, the efficacy of light absorption directly relates to the molar extinction coefficient (). The molar extinction coefficient incorporates two physical quantities: (1) optical cross-section and (2) the probability of absorption (Figure 1.7). The optical cross-section is proportional to the size of the molecule, while the probability directly relates to quantum mechanics and probability of an optical transition. The most probable absorption (SS*) corresponds to a transition between the two electronic states with the best wave function overlap (Frank-Condon Principle).38 The dichotomy here pertains to the polarizability () operator (transition dipole moment). Polarizability represents a molecules propensity to undergo charge redistribution upon photon absorption. However, with greater charge redistribution, there is necessarily less wave function overlap between ground and excited states (Figure 1.6). Therefore, the -spacer is critical for negotiating wave function overlap and it suggests that there is an optimal length and type of -spacer needed in dye design. Furthermore, this suggests that the use of theoretical density functional theory (DFT) calculations, and time dependent (TD-DFT) can be advantageous when designing an effective dye.
8

Figure 1.7. Factors affecting the extinction coefficient ()

1.3.5. Designing organic dyes inspired by Nature The sensitization of metal-free organic dyes was first studied in 1994.39,40 Squaraine dyes (Figure 1.8) were used to study the performance of the DSSC. It was observed that the cell showed low conversion efficiencies of 0.05-0.7%. It was found that the poor conversion efficiencies were due to very high back-electron transfer and very low singlet fluorescence quantum yields of the symmetrical squaraine sensitizers. Since then, different types of organic sensitizers have been studied for application in DSSCs.

Figure 1.8. Squaraine dye [bis(2,4,6-trihydroxyphenyl)squaraine]

Naturally inspired porphyrin-based dyes have long been pursued for their photophysical properties, but recently have been shown to be promising candidates for DSSC applications as well. The intrinsic advantages of porphyrin-based dyes are their rigid molecular structures with large absorption coefficients in the visible region and their many reaction sites capable of fine tuning of the optical, physical, electrochemical properties of porphyrins.41 The rational design of porphyrin dyes involve creating a redox stable push-pull system, which has now led to the highest

9

validated power conversion efficiencies in DSSC dyes; 12%42 (Figure 1.9a) and 13% 7(Figure 1.9b), respectively.

Figure 1.9. Porphyrin-based dyes for DSSC applications

The extensive investigations on porphyrin dyes as light sensitizers is inspired by natural photosynthetic systems. Porphyrins are found in a variety of important biological systems and their functions range from oxygen transfer to electron transfer (cytochrome c, cytochrome oxidase) to energy conversion (chlorophyll during photosynthesis). The propensity of porphyrins to be efficient sensitizers is due to its cavity which can fit a variety of metals.8,41

1.4. Artificial synthesis and more of Nature's inspiration for dye design
Plants require sunlight for photosynthesis. During photosynthesis, CO2 and water are converted into carbohydrates and oxygen by a network of proteins and electron transfer chains, all driven by light, and corresponding proton gradients.43,44 The process of plant-based photosynthesis is illustrated in Figure 1.10.

10

Figure 1.10. Natural photosynthesis; sunlight harvesting by plants

During photosystem II (PSII), a photon is absorbed by chlorophyll and the energy is used to excite an electron. The excited electron is carried by mobile quinone-based carriers and are utilized to reduce NADP+ to NADPH. At the same time the proton gradient is generated in lumen which drives the ATP synthesis. Both the NADPH and ATP are used during the Calvin cycle to generate carbohydrates.45 When contrasting DSSC design with PSII, a DSSC can be envisaged as a simplified mimic to natural photosynthesis. During photosynthesis, an electron is excited (by photon of light) from the reaction center and is replaced by the oxidation of H2O (Figure 1.11a). However, in the case of DSSC the electron gets excited from the HOMO of the dye, and is replaced by the electrolyte (Figure 1.11b). Therefore, much can be garnered and gained by mimicking PSII for DSSC optimization.

11

Figure 1.11. An analogy between harvesting light by plants (a) and DSSC (b)

Another possible DSSC inspiration from photosynthesis is the concept of using multiple redox-active chromophores for energy and electron transfer. Over simplified in the above prose, when the photon is absorbed by PSII it is seldom the reaction centre that absorbs the photon but rather a complex series of antennae dyes. These antenna molecules are porphyrin rings or carotenoids that direct and regulate excitation energy flow (Figure 1.12a). As a result, the absorbed energy gets transferred to the neighboring molecules through the process of Forester resonance energy transfer (FRET); ultimately funneling down toward the reaction centre dimer. This process keeps on going until the energy in the reaction centre is sufficient when finally, an electron is excited to the charge transfer chain (Figure 1.12b). As such, Nature's light-harvesting capabilities depend on supramolecular chromophores for capturing, transferring and converting solar energy.46

12

Figure 1.12. a) The carotenoids and antennae complexes used in the b) Forster resonance energy transfer (FRET) during photosynthesis45

Another key structural feature in this FRET process is that rigid protein frameworks facilitate rapid energy transfer from highly-conjugated antenna complexes to the reaction centre, and based on this inspiration mimicking antenna scaffolds has become a popular pursuit towards solar energy production.47 To this end, there has been considerable interest in developing intensely absorbing rigid dyestuffs capable of mimicking this natural behaviour.

1.5. Phenylacetylene macrocycles
One family of chromophore capable of acting as antennae complexes are the shapepersistent phenylacetylene macrocycles.48 Owing to their intense light absorption, these extended structures have been explored in a number of optoelectronic and light-harvesting applications,49 and the synthesis of large macrocycles with well-defined geometries has garnered considerable interest in the recent years.50-53 Their architectures have similarity to natural light harvesting complexes which can collect light and transfer its energy through excitation energy transfer (EET) processes.54,55 Increasing interest in conjugated macrocycles is attributed to the their promising application in material science,56 in host-guest systems,57 storage devices,58 digital computation,59 molecular rotors.60-62 Compound 1.1 represents a simple phenylacetylene macrocycle (PAM). The size of PAM can be changed from several angstroms to several nanometers, and different functional groups can be
13

incorporated to tune the electronic properties of the macrocycles.63 Owing to their ease of synthesis and self-assembling capabilities, they have become promising building blocks for a variety of supramolecular structures. Acetylene-based macrocycles have also been used for the synthesis of complex rigid macrocyclic scaffolds having chromophores contained within them (vide infra),64,65 and these macrocycles have an interior and exterior that can be substituted with functional groups making them attractive structures for organic materials.66

PAMs can have various conformations due to the existence of ring-strain energy within the molecules. Their rigid backbones give rise to large number of molecular surfaces and often results in well-organized structures. Phenylacetylene macrocycles, with different numbers of phenylacetylene units (ranging from 4 to 9) were studied and it was found that macrocycles with 4, 5 and 6 repeat units adopt only a planar conformation. However, when the size of macrocycle exceeds 6 units, the distorted conformations (boat-like or chair-like) become predominant, being energetically more favored.63

1.5.1. Synthesis of phenylacetylene macrocycles Synthesis of macrocycles is generally carried out using cross-coupling reactions between sp- or sp2- hybridized carbon atoms. This has been done with a variety of palladium cross-coupling reactions like Sonogashira cross-coupling67-69, Negishi cross-coupling70, Heck reaction71 etc. These coupling reactions generally give high yields in individual steps and show high tolerance
14

towards functional groups. A general mechanism of the Sonogashira cross-coupling reactions is shown in Figure 1.13.

Figure 1.13. General mechanism for Sonogashira Cross-Coupling reaction

15

During palladium catalysed cross-coupling, a palladium catalyst begins the cycle as Pd0. Oxidative addition with an aryl halide results in an intermediate species and palladium is converted form Pd0 to PdII. During the transmetallation step, the halide is replaced with a terminal deprotonated alkyne yielding a PdII species. The reductive elimination results in C-C bond formation between the aryl and alkyne carbon and PdII is converted to Pd0 again allowing it to continue into another cycle. In general, the other cross-coupling reactions follow a similar mechanism, and the difference with the Sonogashira reaction is that copper is used as a co-catalyst to lower the pKa of the terminal acetylene, ultimately permitting transmetallation. Although coupling reactions provide a large variety in the synthesis of macrocycles, they also involve a large number of synthetic steps which are kinetically controlled. The ring closing step requires dilute conditions (< 1 mM) and the overall yield in these reaction is often quite low.72,73 The yield of the macrocycle drops with the increasing size of the macrocycle. The low yield is due to the irreversible nature of the reactions so that the undesired bond formation cannot be avoided.64 In order to overcome the problem of low yield, people have used an alternative approach based on dynamic covalent chemistry (DCC), which prepares macrocycles in high yield.74 This approach overcomes the formation of undesired products because of its reversible nature. Examples of this approach are the metathesis reactions (imine metathesis75 and alkyne metathesis76) which, under thermodynamically controlled conditions, have synthesized macrocycles in high yield. Zhang and Moore have summarized the kinetically controlled and thermodynamically controlled synthesis of shape-persistent macrocycles.64

1.5.2. Phenylacetylene macrocycles and energy transfer Macrocycles have the ability to absorb light energy and redistribute the charge density to other parts of the molecule. This could be done either through bonding or through space. The process of light-harvesting and energy transfer was demonstrated by the following study which

16

shows the -conjugated polymeric wires (poly(phenylene-ethynylene-butadiynylene)) covalently encapsulated by conjugated macrocycle.77

Molecule 1.2 is a polymeric species where the polymer chain runs through a covalently bound macrocycle. The energy absorbed by the macrocycle is transferred to and concentrated in the wire core (Figure 1.14). The absorption for the macrocycle polymer (1, c, black line) shows two absorption peaks at 337 and 442 nm which correlates to the absorption of macrocycle (3, a, blue line) and the polymer (2, b, green line). The binding of macrocycle to the polymer could slightly change the individual absorption. The emission of the macrocycle (a, blue dotted line) overlaps with the absorption of polymer 2. Energy transfer occurs and as a result the emission of macrocycle polymer 1 is identical to that of the core polymer 2 and is independent of the excitation wavelength. The temporal rise in the emission of the acceptor provides the ultimate confirmation that the EET did occur within the molecule and that the emission occurs from the core.

17

Figure 1.14. Absorption and emission spectra molecule 1.2, along with its components.77 Figure reproduced with permission from Angewandte Chemie-International Edition 2007, 46, 3450. Copyright 2007 John Wiley and Sons.

1.5.3. Macrocycles and two photon absorption Due to the large conjugation length, phenylacetylene macrocycles can exhibit two photon absorption i.e. two photons are used to excite an electron. In the following study a phenylacetylene

18

macrocycle derivative 1.3 with triphenylamine units was reported.78 Macrocycle 1.3 showed an enlarged two-photon absorption cross-section.

The absorption and emission spectra of three compounds along with the reference TPA are shown in Figure 1.15. All the three compounds show maximum absorption at ~ 380 nm which corresponds to the * transition. Absorption ~ 300 nm relates to that of TPA and it decreases from dimer to tetramer and almost diminishes in case of macrocycle 1.3. This shows complete electron delocalization through TPA and ethynyl segment in 1.3. Also, the maximum absorption shows red-shifting from dimer to macrocycle which corresponds to the extended conjugation length in the molecular backbone. The emission spectra ~430 nm shows a similar trend with slight onset emission redshift from 1 to 3 which can be attributed to the more extended conjugation. The two-photon absorption was measured by a Z-scan technique which uses a femtosecond laser source. Macrocycle 1.3 showed a two-photon absorption cross-section peak at 650 nm which is less than 760 nm (double wavelength of their linear absorption maxima). This could possibly be
19

due to the fact that the two-photon excitation might populate the molecule to a higher energy excited state with larger probability as compared to that by one-photon excitation.78 Compared with regular phenylacetylene macrocycles, 1.3 showed an extended cross-section due to its more branched structure.

Figure 1.15. Absorption and emission of dimer (1), tetramer (2) and macrocycle (1.3), along with the reference triphenylamine.78 Figure reproduced with permission from Tetrahedron Lett. 2012, 53, 4885. Copyright 2012 Elsevier.

In another study, the shape persistent symmetrical structure of phenylacetylene macrocycle was used to synthesis a two-photon absorption macrocycle 1.4.79 The macrocycle had dibutylamino- (Bu2N) and butoxy- (BuO) as the donor groups which increased the longwavelength absorption maxima and the Stokes shift. When compared to the absorption maximum of unsubstituted phenylacetylene macrocycle, 1.4 witnessed an increase of ~110 nm in the absorption maximum which was attributed entirely to the presence of donor groups. Because more rigid fluorophores are typically more fluorescent80 (because rigid structures provide a better wavefunction overlap in excited and ground states), macrocycle 1.4 was shown to be strongly fluorescent. The two-photon excitation spectrum of 1.4 showed a similar pattern except the

20

wavelength was doubled relative to the one photon spectrum which is the evidence that the macrocycle experiences two-absorption absorption.

More recenty, structure-property relationships of acetylene-linked perylene bisimide (PBI) macrocycles were studied.81 The PBI macrocycles were found to be promising candidates for artificial light-harvesting systems. Macrocycles with three to six PBI units (1.5 has three PBI) were synthesized and it was observed that the macrocycles with three and four PBI units were more rigid molecules and they showed a well-defined geometry. With increased ring size the structures became more distorted; the fluorescence lifetimes became shorter and their distributions became broader. On the other hand, the smaller ring size macrocycles (with PBI units 3-4) showed higher

21

photostability and efficient excitation energy transfer (EET). These properties were attributed to their well-defined and planar structures.

1.6. BODIPY dyes
BODIPY (4,4-difluoro-4-bora-3a,4a-diaza-s-indacene) dyes have similarity to porphyrins and can be generally considered as demi-porphyrins. They was first discovered by Treibs and Kreuzer in 1968.82. BODIPY has been used in a variety of advanced material applications due to its highly desirable properties such as high absorption coefficients, sharp emission peaks exhibiting high quantum yields, and chemical stability under wide range of conditions. Their fluorescence characteristics can further be changed by modifying their structures. The generic core and its relative positions are shown in Figure 1.16. Owing to these characteristics, as well as their stability to physiological conditions and insensitivity to the polarity and pH of the surrounding environment83, they have found a wide range of applications in labelling proteins84,85 and DNA83, for imaging events in cells and whole organisms, chemosensors, fluorescent switches, laser dyes, dye sensitized solar cells and organic photovoltaics (OPVs).86
22

Figure 1.16. BODIPY core and its relative positions

1.6.1. General synthesis of BODIPY Dipyrranes are precursors for the synthesis of BODIPYs. The most relevant synthetic methodologies aimed at developing efficient synthetic routes, as well as achieving wider structural diversity of dipyrromethane derivatives, have been summarized in a review by Pereira and coworkers.87 Dipyrranes also constitute important building blocks in the synthesis of porphyrins, corroles, chlorins, calix[4]pyrroles, porphodimethenes, and dipyrrinato complexes. The 2012 review by Gryko et al. explains the most practical and useful synthetic methodologies for the synthesis of the above mentioned compounds starting with dipyrranes.88 Bessette and Hanan86 have focused on summarizing the recent developments in the use of materials based on dipyrromethene and azadipyrromethenes, especially their BF2 derivatives (BODIPY and azaBODIPY), for organic photovoltaic (OPV) applications.86 In general, BODIPY can be prepared using two main synthetic routes: 1) The pyrrole is reacted with an aldehyde through a double electrophilic aromatic substitution and condensation to create a dipyrrolemethane. This step is facilitated by using trifluoroacetic acid (TFA) which creates an electrophilic centre (Scheme 1.1). This intermediate is then oxidized using DDQ or p-chloranil, and reacted with an organic base and boron trifluoride diethyletherate (BF3·OEt2) to form BODIPY.83,89

23

Scheme 1.1. A general scheme for the synthesis of BODIPY using an aldehyde

The other alternative route uses an acid chloride which reacts with pyrrole to make a dipyrrin core that then reacts with a base and BF3·OEt2 to form BODIPY (Scheme 1.2).83,90

Scheme 1.2. General synthetic approach to BODIPY using acid chlorides

1.6.2. Tuning BODIPY The electronic properties of BODIPY can be tuned by substituting its various positions. The meso-position sits on the mirror plane of BODIPY and can be readily modified using different aldehydes and acid chloride derivatives. Unfortunately, while this is the synthetically easiest position to modify, it also has the smallest effect on the optical properties of the dye, because the mirror plane does not significantly interact with the BODIPY -system. The use of aromatic substituents minimally extends conjugation and only a slight broadening of the absorption profile is observed, unless a redox active species is added.91 Bañuelos and co-workers systematically studied modification at the meso-position to test the photochemical properties influenced by
24

substituents on the meso-position. It was observed that the alkyl substituents do not affect the absorption profile significantly; however, there can be a slight blue shift of the max owing to inductive effects of the alkyl substituent increasing the electron density on the meso carbon.91 In addition, the Koivisto group has extensively studied tuning BODIPY using the 2,6positions. The 2,6-positions of BODIPY are the most electronegative positions and can undergo electrophilic addition reactions to yield a variety of derivatives. Modification at the 3, 5-positons has also been extensively studied. Electron donating groups have been attached at the 5-position and a non-linear push-pull system has been developed with the donor at the 5-position of the BODIPY core and acceptor at the 3-position.92 Recently Ziessel group prepared BODIPY derivatives substituted on the 5-position and described unsymmetrical triads constructed from extended BODIPY dyes, diketopyrrolopyrrole (DPP) dyes, with electron donor fragments containing triarylamines. The assemblies are such that each module maintains its individual optical and redox properties. The use of phenyl-alkyne-phenyl or phenylalkyne-thienyl spacer units is favorable for weak electronic interaction between the modules.93 Finally, the boron centre can be substituted by replacing fluoride atoms with various groups. Alkyl groups can be introduced to the boron centre through the use of aryl Grignards or aryl lithiums to displace the fluoride atoms.94-96 Alkoxide substituents can be introduced through aluminum trichloride in the desired alcohol whereas alkyne groups can also be added by using an acetylide anion to displace fluoride.95 Generally speaking the substitution of fluoride with aryl or alkyne groups have the least effect on absorption and emission properties owing to the fact that boron centre does not affect the conjugation on BODIPY core. However, boron substitution has an impact on the fluorescence which decreases by replacing fluoride with bulky alkyl groups.94-96

1.6.3. BODIPYs in photovoltaics One of the limitations of organic dyes is their narrow high-energy absorption bands. In order to improve the DSSC performance, organic dyes should possess panchromatic absorption envelopes extending into the near-infrared (NIR). Encouraged by the excellent photophysical properties and efficient electron transfer in BODIPY dyes, efforts to include them as non-innocent -spacers (which have absorption and emission properties) within the DSSCs dye motif have been growing.97
25

In the first attempts, BODIPY dyes were found to be ineffective sensitizers for DSSC applications.98,99 Akkaya and coworkers investigated a small set of BODIPY dyes possessing Type II (Figure 1.7) D-A motifs.100-103 This was a surprising choice for a first generation dye, because substitution at the meso-position results in poor electronic communication with the BODIPY core, and when D-A motifs are added at the meso-position, energy-transfer "cassettes" typically result; in general energy transfer through the BODIPY core tends to be less efficient in both Type I & II motifs. Conversely, extremely fast energy transfer is observed when transition moments are aligned (Figure 1.17; Type III).90,95,104 Therefore, BODIPY motifs of Type III are more suitable for DSSC applications. One method of incorporating BODIPY into a Type III motif is presented by dyes 1.6 and 1.7.

Figure 1.17. Type I, II and III donor (D)/acceptor (A) motifs in BODIPY dyes for DSSC applications

One of the challenges in working with BODIPY DSSC dyes could be due to poor charge separation in the dominant optical transition.90 However, inclusion of a D-A profile across BODIPY broadens and bathochromically shifts the BODIPYs absorption envelope. The Koivisto group recently reported the use of BODIPY as a non-innocent -spacer in DSSC dyes (1.6).90 Using TPA donors and cyanoacetic acid acceptors at the 2,6 positions lead to a broadening profile and panchromatic light-harvesting, and a significant improvement in device performance. This has been pushed even further via the use of a phenothiazine (PTZ) moiety as electron donor resulting in an efficiency of over 5% (1.7).104

26

1.6.4. Energy transfer in BODIPYs In the previous sections, discussion has focused on the absorption properties of individual chromophores. Although BODIPY exhibit strong absorption properties, it also possesses a relatively narrow absorption window. One strategy to overcome this limitation is to make a dyad chromophore system where BODIPY can be attached to another chromophore. In this dyad assembly, each component absorbs at a different wavelength and upon absorption of light, one chromophore transfers the absorbed energy to the other chromophore. Thus a dyad assembly leads to a panchromatic absorption. As a result, BODIPYs have been used in multi-component molecular arrays that demonstrate electronic energy transfer (EET) over large distances. These molecular arrays feature the positioning of individual units to directed EET along the molecular axis or along another preferred pathway.105 The assembly of two or more BODIPY derivatives have been made and studied for energy transfer. To this end BODIPY triads photosensitizers 1.8106 and 1.9107 exhibited enhanced intramolecular resonance energy transfer. Likewise, diketopyrrolopyrrole -bridges combined with a BODIPY dimer (1.10) permitted the harvesting of low energy photons via intramolecular energy transfer.

27

BODIPY dyes have been substituted with polycyclic aromatic hydrocarbons (PAHs), which in turn transfer energy upon excitation to the BODIPY core. The influence of the orientation between the meso-donor and BODIPY-acceptor on electronic energy transfer has been studied using a molecule with two nearly perpendicular chromophores. This has been achieved by substituting the meso-position with anthracenyl unit108 (1.11), phenanthrene unit109 (1.12), perpendicular oligothiophene donor110 (1.13), -thienyl and furyl donor111 (1.14). A linear molecular dyad was synthesised by attaching a anthracenyl-based unit112 (1.15) and pyrene­ thiophene energy donor113 (1.16) linked via a triazole unit to a BODIPY energy acceptor. The
28

donor to acceptor separation distance is around 20 Å. Emission from the donor originates from a mixed ­* and partial charge-transfer state and overlaps favourably with the absorption profile for the acceptor.

A BODIPY was directly linked to an aza-BODIPY at the -position aza-BODIPY. Owing to the direct linking of the two entities, ultrafast energy transfer was witnessed from BODIPY donor to aza-BODIPY acceptor (1.17).114 Circularly polarized luminescence via intramolecular energy transfer was observed when three moieties having different effects were used: (i) BODIPY acts as the chromophore, (ii) BINOL as the chiral perturbing moiety, and (iii) tetraphenylethene (TPE) as the aggregative induced emission (AIE) and UV-absorbing moiety (1.18).115

29

A recent study combined two energy transfer processes, dark resonance energy transfer (DRET) and fluorescence resonance energy transfer (FRET), in one polarity sensitive donoracceptor system (Figure 1.18).116 The DRET and FRET was studied in organic and aqueous media respectively. In aqueous media, the FRET process was observed. This is because the generation of emissive TPE donor is strongly polarity dependent, due to its aggregation-induced emission (AIE) feature. However, in tetrahydrofuran (THF), the DRET process was observed. This is because TPE fragment of the dye are non-emissive. Photophysical results were similar both in aqueous and organic media with the exception that the TPE fragments of the dye were non-emissive in organic media.

Figure 1.18. Dark resonance energy transfer (DRET) and fluorescence resonance energy transfer (FRET) in one polarity sensitive donor-acceptor system.116 Figure reproduced with permission from Chemistry - A European Journal 2016, 22, 736. Copyright 2016 John Wiley and Sons.

Owing to the large free volume within macrocycles it is possible to bind organic substrates within the cavity and this can influence the properties of both the macrocycle host and guest depending on the nature of the functional groups attached to the macrocycle. Aromatic macrocycles as supramolecular hosts can increase the energy transfer efficiencies owing to their stronger binding affinities to aromatic guests via  stacking117 and hydrophobic effect.118 The Levine group studied the use of aromatic organic macrocycle as supramolecular hosts to observe non-covalent energy transfer.119 They synthesized macrocycle 1.19 that was electronically
30

dissymmetric. The electronic dissymmetry allowed the electron-rich analyte to bind near the electron-deficient component of the macrocycle and an electron-deficient fluorophore was inserted near the electron-rich part of the macrocycle. Benzo[a]pyrene was taken as a donor D and BODIPY as the accepting fluorophore A. The binding was quantified by measuring the changes in the emission spectra of donor molecule (benzo[a]pyrene). An increase in the emission of donor molecule was observed with increasing the concentration of macrocycle 1.19.

Macrocycle 1.19 was also investigated for its ability to promote energy transfer from donor D to fluorescent acceptor A. It was observed that the acceptor emission increased (Figure 1.19A) by adding the donor molecules and that was due to the energy transfer from the donor to the

31

fluorophore acceptor. Also, a decrease in the donor emission was observed when a competing energy acceptor was added (Figure 1.19B).

Figure 1.19. Energy transfer in macrocycle 1.19 (A and B). FDA and FD are the integrated emission of the donor in the presence and absence of acceptors whereas IDA and IA are the integrated emission of the fluorophore in the presence and absence of analyte.119 Figure reproduced with permission from Chem. Commun. 2013, 49, 8259. Copyright 2013 Royal Society of Chemistry.

Inspired by the natural photosynthetic systems, researchers have investigated the BODIPY and porphyrin assemblies in covalent and non-covalent arrays. In these assemblies, light is harvested by more than one chromophore and the excitation energy is transferred to the acceptor unit by the antenna effect. In general, the porphyrins absorbs in 400­450 nm (Soret band) and 550­650 nm (weak to medium Q bands). The inherent problem of porphyrin's poor absorptivity in the blue-green region (450-550 nm) can be overcome by linking it with BODIPY which absorbs strongly in blue-green region (near 500 nm).120-124 The efficiency of the intramolecular energy transfer in antennae assemblies depends on the extent of electronic coupling between the chromophores which could be modulated either by the bridge or the special orientation of the units.125,126 Triazole bridged BODIPY-Zn or BODIPY-Ni porphyrin conjugates containing one BODIPY and one porphyrin (dyad, 1.20127 and 1.21128), three BODIPYs and one porphyrin (tetrad, 1.22128) and four BODIPYs and one porphyrin (pentad, 1.23128) were studied. The absorption spectra shows increase of roughly three and four times in the BODIPY (donor) -* transition. The fluorescence studies showed a strong emission from the porphyrin unit when excited at 495 nm (where the BODIPY absorbs strongly), supporting an
32

energy transfer from BODIPY to the porphyrin unit and the efficiencies were recorded with more than 90%.

33

Both covalent129 (1.24) and non-covalent130 (1.25) architectures incorporating BODIPY, a Zn porphyrin and squaraine units were also studied for panchromatic absorption. By combining the squaraine unit (which absorbs in the region of 600-700 nm) with BODIPY and porphyrin units, the researchers were able to absorb in the entire visible spectrum. In this dye design, the squaraine served as an EWG and anchor to TiO2 nanoparticle in DSSC, while simultaneously the squaraine also serves as a light harvesting chromophore. The BODIPY dye lacking the imidazole moiety (1.24) displayed no enhancement in DSSC performance despite panchromatic absorption, and the -values were higher than that of 1.25. However, the BODIPY dye bearing an imidazole moiety non-covalently bound (1.25) increased the efficiency of the DSSC to 4%; highlighting the importance of the supramolecular assembly for efficient energy transfer.

1.7. Resonance Energy Transfer
Energy transfer (ET) occurs when one molecule or moiety serves to donate energy to another acceptor molecule or molecular substituent. Förster provided the first rationale of this process as the energy released from an excited donor could simultaneously excite the ground-state
34

acceptor based on the Coulombic interaction between these two chemical groups.131 Dexter provided another mechanism by which an excited donor group transfer energy to the acceptor group due to hopping of electrons to accomplish the non-radiative process.132 The processes of Dexter and Forster resonance energy transfer are depicted in Figure 1.20.

Figure 1.20. Dexter (top) and Förster (bottom) processes of energy transfer (ET)

Energy transfer have found wide range of applications in biological systems. The precise location and nature of the interactions between specific molecular species in living cells is of major interest in many areas of biological research.133 Energy transfer have also been extensively studied for material applications e.g. photovoltaics8 (for panchromatic absorption) and light-emitting diodes and fluorescent sensors etc.134 1.7.1. Förster (Fluorescence) Resonance Energy Transfer (FRET)
FRET is non-radiative resonance energy transfer. Unlike observing traditional fluorescence behavior, FRET involves the excitation of the donor (or auxillary chromophore) that transfers its energy "through space" to the acceptor (fluorophore). For FRET to happen, the donor's emission band must have sufficient overlap with the acceptor's adsorption band (Figure 1.21a). Also, the emission dipole of the donor 35

and the absorption dipole of the acceptor must be at the same frequency (wavelength) in order to be in resonance.133,135,136 It is essentially a two-step version of the same excitation and emission process. The energy absorbed by the donor molecule perturbs charge distribution of the acceptor (if the two states are correctly oriented and the molecules are close in space) resulting in the acceptor being excited. This results in the relaxation of donor without emission of a photon and thus radiationless transfer of energy to the acceptor occurs (Figure 1.21b). The acceptor can eventually relax to its ground state by emitting a photon of longer wavelength.133,137,138

Figure 1.21. a) An overlap between emission of the donor and absorbance of the acceptor as a requirement of FRET and b) the mechanism of FRET from donor to acceptor

A typical distance range for FRET is 10-100 Angstroms (Å)138,139 and it is used as a technique to monitors the distance between different fluorescent probes typically attached to macromolecules. FRET can be employed to monitor either binding interactions between different molecules or conformational changes within the same molecule. This technique can be used both in vitro and in vivo.133 The efficiency of energy transfer (ET) is a measure of the fraction of photons absorbed by the donor that are transferred to the acceptor and is evaluated as shown in Equation 1.1. ET = 1 - (TDA/TD) -------------------- Equation 1.1 where TDA is the donor lifetime in the presence of the acceptor and TD is the donor lifetime in the absence of the acceptor. Besides spectral overlap between the donor emission and acceptor absorption spectra and the relative orientation of the donor and acceptor transition dipole moments, the other factors effecting the rate 36

of energy transfer include quantum yield of the donor and the distance separating the donor and acceptor molecules. According to Förster's theory, the rate of energy transfer is given by the Equation 1.2. KT = (1/TD) · [R0/r]6 -------------------- Equation 1.2 where R0 is the Förster critical distance, TD is the donor lifetime in the absence of the acceptor, and r is the distance separating the donor and acceptor chromophores. The Förster critical distance is the maximal separation length between donor and acceptor molecules under which resonance energy transfer will still occur. The value of R0 may be calculated from the following expression given by Equation 1.3. R0 = 2.11 × 10-2 · [2 · J() · -4 · QD]1/6 -------------------- Equation 1.3 where -squared is a factor describing the relative orientation in space between the transition dipoles of the donor and acceptor, J() is the overlap integral in the region of the donor emission and acceptor absorbance spectra (with the wavelength expressed in nanometers),  represents the refractive index of the medium, and QD is the quantum yield of the donor.

1.7.2. Dexter Resonance Energy Transfer (DRET) Dexter energy transfer involves transfer of excitation energy from donor to acceptor due to hopping of electrons via a non-radiative path.132 The Dexter exchange energy transfer is generally associated with fluorescence quenching mechanism (quenching implies any physical process or molecular state that decreases the molecular fluorescence). DRET requires an overlap between the wavefunctions of both donor and acceptor (0/1). This typically requires -resonance interactions between neighbors. The overlap of wavefunctions also implies that the excited donor and ground-state acceptor should be close enough so the exchange could happen. DRET can only occur at short distances and is generally observed within a distance of 10 Å.132,134,140 Dexter energy transfer is a process that two molecules (intermolecular) or two parts of a molecule (intramolecular) bilaterally exchange their electrons (not formally considered electron transfer). The exchange mechanism could be through a singlet-singlet energy transfer (1D* + 1A  1D + 1A*) in which a singlet group produces another singlet group (Figure 1.22a) or through a triplet-triplet energy transfer (3D* + 1A  1D + 3A*) in which a triplet group produces another triplet group (Figure 1.22b).141,142

37

Figure 1.22. Electron exchange mechanism during DRET; a) singlet-singlet energy transfer and b) triplettriplet energy transfer

Even if there are competing mechanisms between Forster and Dexter mechanisms often a supplementary Dexter contribution will go unnoticed. Also the possibility of quenching by electron transfer (depending on the nature of D and A) should not be overlooked, especially in polar solvents that support this process.143

38

1.8. Objectives
In order to mimic photosynthesis and design better organic based dyes for dye-sensitized solar cell (DSSC) applications,144 this research is aimed at combining the phenylacetylene macrocycle and BODIPY motifs to create and explore a family of non-innocent -spacers that could be used in DSSC dye motif for light-harvesting applications. While this thesis does not produce DSSC dyes, the hypothesis is that upon absorption of light, the phenylacetylene macrocycle will transfer energy to the BODIPY core, thereby increasing the effective absorption envelope. The objective can be categorized into four sub-sections. 1. Synthesize a novel -spacer amenable for integration into a DSSC dye (D--A) motif. This involves the synthesis of BODIPY-phenylacetylene macrocycles in which the BODIPY resides inside and aligned orthogonally to the phenylacetylene macrocycle. Alignment of BODIPY orthogonal to the macrocycle is significant because it minimizes the chance of Dexter energy transfer owing to the lack of orbital overlap. As a result, the energy transfer in this structural design is hypothesized to operate through a Förster mechanism (see section 1.7 for details). 2. Explore energy transfer behavior in order to elucidate the potential for desirable energy transfer within the molecule and to ascertain if energy transfer results in enhancing the absorption envelop.
3. Systematically tune the phenylacetylene-BODIPY dyad by adding donor groups to the

macrocycle with the hope of tuning the frontier molecular orbitals, and absorption envelope.
4. Begin synthetic modifications toward DSSC applications where the BODIPY-macrocycle

-spacer would be incorporated within a D--A motif.

39

Chapter 2: BODIPY-Phenylacetylene Macrocycles for Enhanced Light-Harvesting and Energy Transfer Applications
2.1. Introduction
As stated in the thesis objectives, an orthogonal BODIPY-phenylacetylene macrocycle was the idealized structure sought in order to study energy transfer and evaluate the efficacy of this building block as a -spacer for light harvesting applications. The hypothesis is that upon absorption of light, the phenylacetylene macrocycle would effectively transfer energy to the BODIPY core, thereby increasing the effective absorption envelope of the dye. To this end, the synthesis, characterization and evaluation of physicochemical properties of an orthogonal phenylacetylene macrocycle-BODIPY dyad are described herein. A proposed design of the target BODIPY-macrocycle is shown in Figure 2.1, where the BODIPY is tethered and oriented orthogonally within the cavity of the macrocycle. To make this proposed target molecule, a variety of strategies were attempted and employed. To this end, a symmetric macrocycle can be broken down into four parts: two arms, one top BODIPY containing unit, and a bottom cap (Figure 2.1b). The top BODIPY unit should have structural features capable of connecting the phenylacetylene arms. The phenylacetylene arms are intended to extend the conjugation and provide a large enough cavity to support the BODIPY unit and close the ring using the bottom cap.

Figure 2.1. A proposed design of the orthogonal BODIPY-phenylacetylene macrocycle (a) and its assembly from building blocks (b) 40

Subsections 2.2.1 ­ 2.2.3 of this thesis describe the synthesis of the various building blocks designed for the construction of a BODIPY-phenylacetylene macrocycle and the attempted synthesis using the "bottom cap-up" (2.2.4) approach. Owing to synthetic limitations, a top BODIPY-down approach was employed (2.2.6), which required the synthesis of new building blocks (2.2.5), but ultimately led to the formation of a desirable BODIPY-phenylacetylene macrocycle dyad and derivatives (vide infra).

41

2.2. Synthesis
2.2.1. A retrosynthetic analysis of BODIPY-phenylacetylene macrocycle A retrosynthetic approach for the synthesis of BODIPY-phenylacetylene macrocycle is proposed in Scheme 2.1. According to this approach, the small building blocks (2.15 and 2.19) are reacted to yield TMS-protected U-shape molecule (2.20) which is then deprotected (2.21) and reacted with desired BODIPY-cap (2.9) to yield the desired product (2.22). The building blocks are selected such that the BODIPY is enclosed in the macrocycle.

Scheme 2.1. A retrosynthetic approach for the synthesis of BODIPY-Phenylacetylene macrocycle 42

2.2.2. Synthesis of top BODIPY cap The first step towards the synthesis of the BODIPY cap was to make an appropriate aldehyde. As such, 4-tert-butyl-2,6-diiodobenzaldehyde (2.4) was synthesized using a modified literature method.145 The individual steps starting from 4-tert-butyltoluene (which is commercially available) are summarized in Scheme 2.2. Substitution of 4-tert-butyltoluene with iodine by electrophilic aromatic substitution produced compound 2.1, which was then oxidized with KMnO4 to yield acetate 2.2. The acetate was hydrolyzed to the alcohol 2.3, which was oxidized to the corresponding benzaldehyde 2.4 using Pyridinium chlorochromate (PCC). The overall yield of 2.4 over the 4 steps was 36%.

Scheme 2.2. Synthesis of substituted benzaldehyde 2.4

The next step in the preparation of the BODIPY-cap was the preparation of a substituted pyrrole i.e. 2,4-dimethyl-1H-pyrrole (2.6). Ethylacetoacetate was converted to a diester 2.5 using the Paul-Knorr synthesis, followed by a decarboxylation of 2.5 using KOH to yield 2.6 according to Scheme 2.3. Although 2.6 has been previously reported146, this sequence has been optimized to be more effective. The separation of the pyrrole in the presence of ethylene glycol is achieved

43

using vacuum distillation at 60 oC over a period of 3 hours. The overall yield of the product over two steps was 43%.

Scheme 2.3. Synthesis of Pyrrole 2.6

The oxidation of benzaldehyde 2.4 to benzoic acid 2.7 was attempted with the hope that it could be converted to the acid chloride 2.8 (because the synthesis of BODIPY from acetyl chloride yield high). The acid chloride could then be reacted with pyrrole 2.6 to yield BODIPY 2.9 according to Scheme 2.4. However, this reaction failed en route to 2.7. One fraction was recovered as the starting material whereas, the rest of the fractions were some unidentified intermediates. One of the potential reasons for this failed reaction could be the purification and isolation. The purification of carboxylic acid using chromatography could have been a determining factor as carboxylic acids have a strong affinity for the stationary phase.

Scheme 2.4. Expected oxidation of 2.4 to the corresponding benzoic acid, acetyl chloride and BODIPY

44

After a failed attempt to convert aldehyde to acetyl chloride, the aldehydes 2.4 was reacted with pyrrole 2.6 to make BODIPY (Scheme 2.5). Following a protocol for a similar BODIPY,147 the reaction conditions were adjusted to prepare BODIPY derivative 2.9.

Scheme 2.5. Synthesis of BODIPY 2.9

This novel compound 2.9 was fully characterized by 1H NMR, 13C NMR, 11B NMR, 19F NMR, and mass spectrometry. This high symmetry compound (2.9) has a simple NMR spectrum (Figure 2.2). There are two sets of methyl protons (b and c) and a set of tert-butyl protons in the aliphatic region. One set of methyl protons (c) is strongly shielded which could be due to the orthogonal phenyl ring. There is also a set of two equivalent protons in the aromatic region. Integration gives the exact number of protons for BODIPY 2.9. The NMR spectra (13C NMR, 11B NMR and 13C NMR) can be found in appendices.

2.2.4. Synthesis of bottom-cap The synthesis of the next building block (bottom-cap 2.19) is elaborated in Scheme 2.9 and the sequence had an overall yield of 31%. To carry out the synthesis according to the bottom-up approach, both building blocks 2.15 and 2.19 were reacted together via a Sonogashira cross-coupling to yield product 2.20 as outlined in Scheme 2.10. This TMS-protected derivative 2.20 was characterized using 1H NMR and
13

C

NMR spectroscopy. The 1H NMR (given in appendices) did not provide conclusive information as the aromatic protons have significant overlap. However, the TMS protons and aromatic protons were integrated according to the structure. 13C NMR resolved clean enough to show all 25 distinct carbons. Also, the high resolution mass spectral data provided evidence for this compound as the experimental mass was consistent with the calculated one. The next step was the deprotection of the terminal TMS groups to get compound 2.21. Also, the appearance of two terminal alkyne protons at 3.10 ppm in the 1H NMR and the disappearance of the TMS protons suggested the formation of the terminal alkyne derivative 2.21.

Scheme 2.10. Synthesis of pre-macrocycle U-shape molecule 2.21

The next, and perhaps the most critical, step was the synthesis of the target BODIPYmacrocycle 2.22, depicted in Scheme 2.11. The alkyne derivative 2.21 was reacted with BODIPY 2.9 through a Sonogashira cross-coupling to afford the BODIPY-macrocycle 2.22. This reaction was done under high dilution in order to avoid competitive polymerization. However, this reaction failed to yield the desired product. This failure was believed to be linked to steric crowding of the methyl groups on BODIPY 2.9, and the Sonogashira cross-coupling being too demanding at the reaction centre. A few fractions were obtained but none of them were characterized as the starting material or the product.

50

Scheme 2.11. Synthesis of BODIPY macrocycle 2.22

To check the validity of this hypothesis, the same reaction was attempted with ethynyl benzene instead of 2.21 (Scheme 2.12). While the steric demands were similar to that of the macrocycle formation, the number of bond forming reactions was also similar when using ethynyl benzene. Again this reaction failed to show any indication of coupling at all. This led us to conclude that sterics proximate to the BODIPY core were prohibiting the coupling reaction.

Another strategy to minimize steric hindrance was to move the demanding coupling reaction farther from the BODIPY core. To this end, TMS-acetylene was reacted with BODIPY 2.24 to yield 2.25 according to Scheme 2.14. At first, this reaction was carried out using route A (Scheme 2.14) but the overall yield over the last two steps (to make 2.24 and 2.25) was 7.7%. This lower yield was mainly due to the first step, because the synthesis of BODIPY is typically a low yielding reaction. To improve the yield during these steps, an alternative attempt was made by reacting di-iodobenzaldehyde 2.4 with TMS-acetylene to yield the TMS-protected benzaldehyde 2.26 which was then reacted with pyrrole to yield the TMS-protected BODIPY 2.25. The overall yield using this route (illustrated by route B, Scheme 2.14) was 26.4%, which became the preferred method of making 2.25.

2.2.8. Characterization of BODIPY-macrocycle The product 2.31 was further purified by column chromatography and 1H NMR spectroscopy showed 12 peaks in the aromatic region with each peak representing two equivalent protons (24 protons in total). Also, tert-butyl protons were integrated for nine protons as shown in Figure 2.6. This integration justifies all the protons in 2.31. The appendices) also satisfies the number of carbons in 2.31.
13

C NMR spectrum (shown in

58

Figure 2.6. 1H NMR of BODIPY-macrocycle 2.31 in CDCl3

The final confirmation of the BOBIPY-macrocycle (2.31) was achieved by x-ray crystallography (Figure 2.7; single crystals were grown from saturated DCM:Hex (1:2) solutions). In the solid state, the dihedral angle between the BODIPY and attached phenyl ring is 118, suggesting that the two chromophores are not truly orthogonal. However, in solution 1H-NMR displayed equivalent environments throughout the macrocycle and BODIPY suggesting that there is a significant amount of rotational freedom for the BODIPY within the cavity, but not enough for it to adopt a coplanar conformation within the macrocycle (vide infra, DFT).

59

Figure 2.7. Crystal structure of BODIPY-macrocycle 2.31. (ORTEP, 30% thermal ellipsoids). Hydrogen atoms and solvent molecules have been removed for clarity

2.2.9. Proposed mechanism for the synthesis of BODIPY-phenylacetylene macrocycle The product 2.31 was likely formed during the initiation step of an attempted Sonogashira cross-coupling reaction. PdCl2(PPh3)2 was used as Pd source; the Pd2+ state needs to undergo reductive elimination before it enters the catalytic cycle. During this initiation step, the macrocycle was likely formed. While a thorough study of the mechanism was not carried out, a proposed mechanism for the cyclization of macrocycle during the initiation step is illustrated in Figure 2.8 whereas the general mechanism for the Sonogashira cross-coupling reaction is discussed in chapter 1 (Figure 1.13).

60

Figure 2.8. Proposed mechanism for the synthesis of product during the initiation step of Sonogashira cross-coupling reaction.

In the case where PdCl2(PPh3)2 is used as a catalyst, Pd is in its PdII state. Both of the Clions are labile, Pd can form a bond with the terminal alkyne in the presence of a base. Both the terminal alkynes come off during the reductive elimination step and form a homodimer. This is likely the reason for getting a five membered macrocycle rather than the originally desired intermolecular product. Kinetics favour this step rather than the cross-coupling reaction and the iodo-derivative never gets a chance to react with the terminal alkyne (Figure 2.8).
61

This proposed mechanism also makes sense in terms of product yield. Because 13-15 mole percent of the Pd catalyst was used, the product had a similar yield. If in fact this is the case, then the presence of di-iodo derivative 2.17 is not required. This hypothesis is further discussed in Section 2.2.10).

2.2.10. Synthesis of control macrocycle In order to conduct energy transfer studies, a control macrocycle 2.34 was needed and synthesized. This control macrocycle has similar structural features to 2.31 except missing the BODIPY. The terminal alkyne derivative 2.19 and arm 2.15 were reacted through a Sonogashira cross-coupling reaction to yield a TMS-protected pre-macrocycle derivative 2.32 followed by deprotection of the TMS groups to yield 2.33. Cyclization of 2.33 was carried out using the same strategy as for the parent BODIPY-macrocycle 2.31 and a control macrocycle 2.34 was successfully synthesized. The reaction sequence is shown in Scheme 2.18.

62

Scheme 2.18. Synthesis of control macrocycle 2.34

In this instance, di-iodo derivative 2.17 was added to keep the procedure exactly the same as the one used for making BODIPY-phenylacetylene macrocycle 2.31. The yield (17%) was similar to the parent macrocycle 2.31.

2.2.11. Synthesis of more rigid BODIPY-macrocycle: back to tetramethyl BODIPY According to the X-ray crystal data of BODIPY-macrocycle 2.31, the BODIPY was inside the macrocycle but not exactly orthogonal to the macrocycle. This observation led us to make a more sterically hindered BODIPY, one that was previously attempted using the bottom-up approach. In this instance, tetramethyl BODIPY 2.36 was required rather than the unsubstituted
63

BODIPY 2.27 (Scheme 2.19). The synthesis was similar to the one employed to make the parent BODIPY-macrocycle 2.31. A tetramethyl BODIPY derivative (2.36) was made as a top BODIPY unit. To achieve 3.6, dimethyl pyrrole 2.6 was reacted with TMS-protected aldehyde 2.26 to produce the TMS-acetylene derivative of BODIPY (2.35) which was then deprotected using KF to yield a terminal alkyne BODIPY 2.36.

Scheme 2.19. Synthesis of alkyne derivative of tetramethyl BODIPY 2.36

The next step was to make the rigid BODIPY-macrocycle 2.39 using BODIPY 2.36, and the phenylacetylene arm 2.15 to yield 2.37, followed by the deprotection of 2.37 to yield 2.38. This time, the last step (cyclization of macrocycle) was carried out without the addition of the diiodo-tert-butyl phenyl unit 2.17 to prove our previous hypothesis (i.e. the reaction does not require the presence of di-iodo derivative 2.17). According to expectations, the cyclization step yielded BODIPY-macrocycle 2.39. A similar yield was obtained for this BODIPY-macrocycle 2.39. Scheme 2.20 shows the synthesis of the rigid BODIPY-phenylacetylene macrocycle 2.39. In addition, it was pleasing that moving the coupling reaction away from the BODIPY core permitted the opportunity to synthesize the original methylated BODIPY target.

64

Scheme 2.20. Synthesis of BODIPY-macrocycle with tetramethyl BODIPY 2.39

2.2.12. Other synthetic attempts towards making BODIPY-phenylacetylene macrocycles This section explains additional syntheses that were explored towards making BODIPYphenylacetylene macrocycles. When previously discussed syntheses were not working, an attempt was made to prepare a macrocycle with an aldehyde group inside its cavity with the hope that this aldehyde could be converted to BODIPY at a later stage. Towards this end, 2.26 was deprotected to yield 2.40 which was then reacted with arm 2.15 to yield the TMS-protected U-shape molecule 2.41 as shown in Scheme 2.21. The deprotection step (to yield 2.40) worked well (spectroscopic data is given in the appendices). However, the next step which was the Sonogashira cross-coupling between the deprotected aldehyde 2.40 and the arm 2.15 did not produce any desired product. Six
65

different fractions were collected but none of them were the product. Most of the fractions showed peaks in the aromatic region but the aldehyde proton was missing.

Scheme 2.21. Attempted synthesis of aldehyde 2.41

An alternative route was explored to make the aldehyde bearing macrocycle (Scheme 2.22). According to this approach, the TMS-protected aldehyde 2.26 was first protected to get 2.42, which was then deprotected to 2.43, which was then reacted with arm 2.15 to yield the protected version of 2.41. A pure fraction of 2.44 was obtained with a 79% yield which was then deprotected to yield 2.45. All of these steps yielded the products in good yield. NMR data of all these protected products (2.42, 2.43, 2.44 and 2.45) are presented in the appendices.

66

Scheme 2.22. Synthesis of protected aldehyde derivative of terminal alkyne 2.45

A macrocycle from 2.45 was never pursued beyond this step for several reasons. Firstly, because this route involved more steps with the most critical (typically lowest yielding) step remaining, it was deemed a less desirable route. Secondly, because the deprotection of the aldehyde involve reflux, the macrocycle might decompose at high temperature. Lastly, the BODIPY-macrocycle was successfully synthesized while this route was being pursued. Despite this route never being used to make the BODIPY-macrocycle, it still could serve as an alternative approach for future studies.

67

2.3. Results and Discussion
2.3.1. Photophysical properties of controlled BODIPY and controlled macrocycle Absorption and emission data were collected in DCM. The control BODIPY (2.24) has a maximum absorption at 515 nm and when excited at its maximum absorption, emission is observed at 533 nm (Figure 2.9a). When the control macrocycle (2.34) is excited at 287 nm (maximum absorption wavelength), the emission is observed at 333 nm (Figure 2.9b).

Figure 2.9. Absorption (blue solid line) and emission (orange dotted line) of BODIPY 2.24 (a) and macrocycle 2.34 (b).

2.3.2. Photophysical properties of BODIPY-phenylacetylene macrocycle 2.31 Absorption data collected in DCM solutions and time-dependent density functional theory (TD-DFT) assigned transitions (labelled according to orbitals in Figure 2.11) are provided in Figure 2.10. Similar to most BODIPY derivatives, building block 2.24, has a strong absorption at 515 nm, and a hypsochromic shoulder; the remainder of the spectrum is relatively featureless. In 2.31, these BODIPY absorptions are slightly blue-shifted (510 nm), and a series of strong absorptions owing to the macrocycle are present below 350 nm.

68

Figure 2.10. Absorption spectra of BODIPY-phenylacetylene macrocycle, 2.31 and benchmark BODIPY 2.24, in DCM. Dominant optical transitions have been assigned using TD-DFT

DFT calculations (B3LYP, 6-31G) of the frontier molecular orbitals (FMOs) for 2.31 are presented in Figure 2.11. The minimized DFT structure possesses a slightly bowl-shaped

phenylacetylene macrocycle, consistent with the observed structure in the solid-state X-ray crystal. Unlike the solid-state structure, the optimized DFT structure sees the BODIPY essentially orthogonal to the attached phenyl ring with a dihedral angle of 89.97. In addition, most FMOs predominantly have electron density on the macrocycle, except the HOMO-2 and HOMO-1 which have significant contributions localised on the BODIPY portion of the molecule, and the LUMO is localised exclusively on the BODIPY.

69

Figure 2.11. DFT calculated frontier molecular orbitals for BODIPY-macrocycle, 2.31

TD-DFT calculations were performed to elucidate the nature of the observed absorption behaviour. Consistent with the assignments in Figure 2.9, the largest optical transition (292 nm) was assigned as the HOMO to LUMO+2. This absorption is localized on the macrocycle and these intense * absorptions arise from optical transitions that possess significant electron density overlap between the ground and excited state. All optical transitions below 350 nm are dominated by strong * absorptions localised predominantly on the macrocycle. Absorptions centred at 510 nm, are due to optical transitions that are primarily localized on the BODIPY (HOMO-1 to LUMO), and are consistent with previous studies.151

2.3.3. Energy transfer in BODIPY-macrocycle Emission data of the BODIPY-macrocycle 2.31 was also collected in DCM and is presented in Figure 2.12. When comparing the emission of the parent BODIPY-macrocycle 2.31 to the control macrocycle 2.34, distinct differences emerge with respect to the fluorophore. Upon
70

excitation of 2.34 at 287 nm (Figure 2.9b), emission from the macrocycle results in a Stokes shift of 46 nm. Conversely, when 2.31 is excited at 292 nm, emission appears to originate from the BODIPY portion of the molecule resulting in a `Stokes-like' shift of 236 nm (Figure 2.12). Irrespective of the excitation wavelength (292, 487 or 510 nm) an emission from BODIPY and proportional to the absorption extinction coefficient, is observed at 528 nm. This is consistent with Kasha's rule (which states that emission is always from the lowest excited state and is thus at longer wavelength as compared to absorption wavelength) where one would expect fluorescence from the lowest excited state, (i.e. the LUMO which is located predominantly on the BODIPY portion of the molecule).

Figure 2.12. Absorption (blue solid line) and emission (dotted lines, orange when excited at 292 nm, black when excited at 487 nm and green when excited at 510 nm) of macrocycle 2.31

Besides the emission scan (Figure 2.12), an excitation scan of 2.31 was run by choosing the emission wavelength at 528 nm. As shown in Figure 2.13, excitation data of 2.31 resembles its absorption profile.

71

Figure 2.13. Absorption and excitation (emission observed @ 528 nm) spectra of 2.31 in DCM

Presumably when light is absorbed by molecule 2.31, the macrocycle part of the molecule is excited, which in turn transfers its absorbed energy to the BODIPY core. Emission from BODIPY irrespective of the excitation wavelength throughout the absorption profile suggests that there is an energy transfer from the macrocycle to the BODIPY core. These results are consistent with other previously reported oligomeric phenylacetylene architectures, where exciton accumulation was observed in conjugated wires sheathed by phenylacetylene macrocycles, despite the wire not being conjugated to the macrocycle.77 More recently, energy transfer has been observed in coordination driven structures with BODIPYs installed on the periphery.152 Using non-covalent host-guest complexes, it was shown that highly efficient energy transfer is observed from a guest to the BODIPY containing host.153 Combining these popular phenylacetylene macrocycle and BODIPY motifs, our work demonstrates that effective supramolecular energy transfer can occur between these intensely absorbing chromophores. Moreover, this motif should be suitable as a spacer for DSSC applications as the orthogonal nature of our structure has ideal energy transfer from the macrocycle to the BODIPY core of the molecule and could be modified for panchromatic absorption.
72

2.3.4. Emission profile of 2.31 at variable concentrations The BODIPY-phenylacetylene macrocycle (2.31) shows energy transfer from the macrocycle to the BODIPY core as can be seen upon excitation of the macrocyclic portion of the compound, and an emission characteristic of BODIPY (Figure 2.12). To eliminate coconcentration dependence as a variable on the emission profile, the emission data at different concentrations of 2.31 was collected. It was observed that intensity of the emission is directly proportional to the concentration (Figure 2.14) suggesting no significant concentration behavior. In Figure 2.14, a blue solid line represents the absorption profile of BODIPY-macrocycle 2.31 and the dotted lines represents the emission data for different concentrations of 2.31 (with the concentrations decreasing from 1 to 4).

Figure 2.14. Absorption spectrum (solid blue line) and emission spectra (dotted lines) of 2.31 with decreasing concentrations from 1-4

73

2.3.5. Absorption/Emission studies of rigid BODIPY-macrocycle 2.39 Photophysical studies were performed on 2.39 and are summarized in Figure 2.15. The absorption profile of 2.39 was similar to that of 2.31 with the exception that the molar extinction co-efficient () of the BODIPY portion was much higher (60000 M-1cm-1) than the one in the parent BODIPY-macrocycle 2.31 (40000 M-1cm-1). This increase in -value is probably due to the fact that the BODIPY containing four methyl groups, is more rigid and orthogonal to the macrocycle and hence leads to higher absorption. The emission data is represented by the dotted line in Figure 2.15. The BODIPYmacrocycle 2.39 was excited at different wavelengths (290 nm, 306 nm, 335 nm, and 510 nm) but the excitation was only observed from the BODIPY core, regardless of the excitation wavelength. This also supports the occurrence of energy transfer within the molecules.

Figure 2.15. Absorption (blue solid line) and emission (dotted lines, orange when excited at 290 nm, black when excited at 306 nm, pink when excited at 335 nm and green when excited at 510 nm) of BODIPY-macrocycle 2.39 74

Absorption and emission data for BODIPY-macrocycles (2.31 and 2.39) and the control subunits (2.24 and 2.34) along with their respective fluorescence quantum yields is summarized in Table 2.1. The fluorescence quantum yield for parent BODIPY unit 2.24 was low (0.12) and the control macrocycle 2.34 emitted at 333 nm. However, when they were attached together to make a BODIPY-phenylacetylene, they were highly fluorescent and exhibited excellent fluorescence quantum yields (0.89 and 0.91 for 2.31 and 2.39 respectively).
