75

2.4. Summary and Conclusion
In this chapter a BODIPY-phenylacetylene macrocycle was synthesized where BODIPY is enclosed in and orthogonal to the phenylacetylene macrocycle. Various synthetic strategies were used to make the target molecule 2.31. However, a successful synthetic strategy (top-down approach) showed that cyclizing the ring at the bottom was a more effective way to close the ring. Also the steric effect on BODIPY does not allow the Sonogashira cross-coupling reaction to take place in the case that the ring is closed at the top (bottom-up approach). After the synthesis of the parent BODIPY-phenylacetylene macrocycle 2.31, the control macrocycle 2.34 was made to carry out the energy transfer studies. Upon excitation at 287 nm, the control macrocycle 2.34 emits at 333 nm with a Stokes shift of 46 nm. However, when the BODIPY-phenylacetylene macrocycle 2.31 is excited at max (292 nm), the emission is from the BODIPY core and a Stokes shift of 236 nm is observed. It is also observed that emission always occurs from the BODIPY core, irrespective of the excitation wavelength which suggests that there is an energy transfer from the macrocycle to the BODIPY core. The process of energy transfer from the macrocycle to BODIPY is depicted in Figure 2.15.

Figure 2.16. An energy transfer process within the BODIPY-macrocycle

A more rigid BODIPY-phenylacetylene macrocycle 2.39 was made which also showed energy transfer from the macrocyclic part to the BODIPY core. The BODIPY core in 2.39 is more orthogonal to the macrocycle as compared to 2.31 which is attributed to the steric effect of the tetramethyl groups on BODIPY in the case of 2.39. It was also observed that both of the BODIPYphenylacetylene macrocycles 2.31 and 2.39 exhibit excellent fluorescence quantum yields (0.89 and 0.91 respectively).
76

The BODIPY-phenylacetylene macrocycle design possesses a macrocycle orthogonal to the BODIPY unit and upon excitation, energy transfer is observed from the macrocyclic part of the compound to the BODIPY core. However, this dye design was intended to be used for light harvesting applications for which it should be able to absorb in the visible region of the solar spectrum. The BODIPY core absorbs around 500 nm but the macrocycle absorbs around 300 nm. This led the study to modify the macrocyclic part of the BODIPY-phenylacetylene to red-shift its absorption profile. To this end, modified BODIPY-phenylacetylene macrocycles were studied as discussed in Chapter 3.

77

2.5. Experimental
2.5.1. General considerations Unless stated otherwise, all reagents were purchased from Sigma Aldrich except PdCl2 (Pressure Chemical Co., Pittsburg, PA). PdCl2 was reacted with PPh3 to make catalyst PdCl2(PPh3)2. Purification by column chromatography was carried out using silica (Silicycle: ultrapure flash silica). Analytical thin-layer chromatography was performed on aluminum-backed sheets precoated with silica 60 F254 adsorbent (0.25 mm thick; Silicycle) and visualized under UV light. Routine 1H, 13C{1H}, 11B{1H}. and 19F NMR spectra were recorded at 400, 100, 128 and 376 MHz, respectively, on a Bruker AV 400 instrument at ambient temperature. Chemical shifts () are reported in parts per million (ppm) from low to high field and referenced to a residual nondeuterated solvent (CHCl3) for 1H and
13

C nuclei and boron trifluoride diethyletherate

(BF3·OEt2) (11B nucleus;  = 0 ppm), C6F6 (19F nucleus;  = 0 ppm). Standard abbreviations indicating multiplicity are used as follows: s = singlet; d = doublet; q = quartet and br = broad. High resolution mass spectrometry (HRMS) results were obtained from Queens University, Kingston Ontario. Electron impact (EI) mass spectrometry and Electrospray ionization (ESI) techniques were used for the ionization; time of flight (TOF) was used for analysis. UV-Vis data was taken using Cary Series UV-Vis-NIR Spectrophotometer from Agilent Technologies and DCM (having the onset peak at 230 nm) was used as the solvent. The GAUSSIAN 09 computational package154 was used to perform ground-state geometry optimization calculations employing Becke's three-parameter hybrid exchange functional and the Lee­Yang-Parr non-local correlation functional B3LYP155-157 and 6-31G basis set was used for all the atoms. Timedependent density functional theory calculations were also performed using this methodology, and the first 200 singlet excited states were calculated. Calculations by the first-principles method were used to obtain accurate excitation energies and oscillator strengths. The solvent was modelled with the polarizable continuum model using DCM as the solvent.158

2.5.2. Synthesis The synthetic procedure of each compound is given below. General synthetic steps have been used unless otherwise mentioned in the procedure.

2,6-diiodo-4-tert-butyltoluene (2.1). In a 500 mL round bottom flask, 4-tert-butyltoluene (4.74 g, 32.0 mmol), KIO4 (7.36 g, 32.0 mmol), I2 (15.0 g, 59.0 mmol), AcOH (40 mL), CHCl3 (30 mL), H2SO4 (12 mL), and H2O (12 mL) were taken and the mixture was heated to 90oC for 24 h. The reaction mixture was then cooled to room temperature (RT) and extracted with Hex:Et2O (1:1). Washing the organic phase with a saturated sodium bicarbonate (NaHCO3) solution followed by removal of solvent (in vacuo) gave product 2.1 as a light orange oil (10.25 g, 80 %). The spectroscopic data agreed with the literature.159 1H NMR (CDCl3, 400 MHz):  = 7.84 (s, 2H), 2.73 (s, 3H), 1.29 (s, 9H). 13C NMR (CDCl3, 100 MHz):  152.39, 139.82, 136.83, 99.53, 34.53, 34.01, 31.16.

4-(tert-butyl)-2,6-diiodobenzyle acetate (2.2). To a stirred solution of 2.1 (4.85 g, 12.1 mmol) in H2SO4 (3.3 mL), AcOH (20 mL), and Ac2O (40 mL), 2.49 g (15.8 mmol) of KMnO4 were slowly added over a period of 3 hours while keeping the temperature at 40 oC. The mixture was stirred for 1 day at the same temperature. The mixture was poured into water and extracted with Et2O. The organic layer was washed with 10% NaOH solution and then with brine. The organic phase was dried over anhydrous MgSO4, filtered and the solvent was removed under reduced pressure. The residue was chromatographed on silica gel using Hex:DCM (1:1) as the eluent to give 4-tert79

butyl-2,6-diiodobenzyl acetate (2.2) as a yellow viscous liquid (3.30 g, 60%). The spectroscopic data agreed with the literature.145 1H NMR (CDCl3, 400 MHz):  = 7.86 (s, 2H), 5.40 (s, 2H), 2.13 (s, 3H), 1.28 (s, 9H).

(4-(tert-butyl)-2,6-diiodophenyl)methanol (2.3). To a stirred solution of the acetate 2.2 (1.39 g, 3.14 mmol) in MeOH (25 mL) was slowly added K2CO3 (0.122 g, 1.05 mmol) and the mixture was stirred at RT for 3 hours. The reaction mixture was quenched by the addition of 2 M HCl. The mixture was extracted with Et2O and the organic fraction was washed with brine, dried over MgSO4, filtered and the solvent was removed under reduced pressure to yield product 2.3 as a yellow viscous liquid (1.10 g, 84%). The spectroscopic data agreed with the literature.145 1H NMR (CDCl3, 400 MHz):  = 7.82 (s, 2H), 5.00 (d, 2H), 2.10 (t, 1H), 1.27 (s, 9H).

4-(tert-butyl)-2,6-diiodobenzaldehyde (2.4). To a stirred solution of alcohol 2.3 (1.03 g, 2.48 mmol) in DCM (20.0 mL) was added PCC (1.54 g, 7.20 mmol) and the mixture was stirred overnight at RT. The solvent was removed under reduced pressure and the residue was chromatographed using Hex:DCM (1:1) as the eluent to give product 2.4 as a yellow solid (0.91 g, 89%). The spectroscopic data agreed with the literature.145 1H NMR (CDCl3, 400 MHz):  = 9.76 (s, 1H), 8.00 (s, 2H), 1.32 (s, 9H).

80

Diethyl-3,5-dimethyl-1H-pyrrole-2,4-dicarboxylate (2.5). Ethyl acetoacetate (20.0 mL, 154 mmol) was added to glacial AcOH (70 mL) and the solution was cooled in an ice bath. In another flask, a solution of NaNO2 (12.2 g, 177 mmol) in H2O (20 mL) was cooled in an ice bath and added drop wise to the first mixture, maintaining an internal temperature of 6­10 oC. The resulting yellow solution was stirred overnight at RT and ethyl acetoacetate (23.0 mL, 178 mmol) was added to the mixture. Zinc powder (22.6 g, 344 mmol) was obtained and a minimum amount was added in small portions to increase the internal temperature of the mixture to 65 oC. The mixture was then cooled to 0 oC and the ice bath was removed. Zinc powder was added in small portions to increase the internal temperature of the mixture to 70 oC and the remaining Zn was added slowly maintaining the internal temperature between 60­80 oC. After the last addition of zinc powder, the mixture was refluxed for 24 hours and poured onto ice. The solids were filtered and washed with H2O. The crude material was recrystallized from hot EtOH, affording product 2.5 as an off-white solid (29.7 g, 80%). The spectroscopic data agreed with the literature.160 1H NMR (CDCl3, 400 MHz):  = 9.09 (s, 1H), 4.30 (q, 4H), 2.56 (s, 3H), 2.51 (s, 3H), 1.37 (t, 6H).

2,4-dimethyl-1H-pyrrole (2.6). Under an atmosphere of N2, pyrrole-dicarboxylate 2.5 (15.0 g) was dissolved in ethylene glycol (~75.0 mL) and KOH (11.7 g, 156.7 mmol) was slowly added. The mixture was heated to 160 oC for 3 hours. After being cooled to room temperature, the product was distilled off under vacuum, affording product 2.6 as an oxygen sensitive dark oil (3.22 g, 54%). The spectroscopic data agreed with the literature.146 1H NMR (CDCl3, 400 MHz):  = 7.62 (s, 1H), 6.41 (s, 1H), 5.76 (s, 1H), 2.24 (s, 3H), 2.09 (s, 3H).

106

Chapter 3: Synthesis of Modified BODIPY-Phenylacetylene Macrocycles Designed to Tune the Absorption Profile
3.1. Introduction
As discussed in the previous chapter, a BODIPY-phenylacetylene macrocycle (2.31) was successfully made with a BODIPY contained within the macrocycle. Also, fluorescence studies showed that there was energy transfer from the macrocycle to the BODIPY. The macrocyclic part of the molecule (2.31) absorbs light in the UV region with a maximum absorption at 292 nm. However, for DSSC applications this is less than ideal, because few photons penetrate our atmosphere at this wavelength. To design a dye for DSSC applications, it needs to be modified such that the macrocyclic part absorbs appreciably in the visible (or near IR)161 (i.e. a red-shift in the absorption profile is required). The solar spectrum and photon flux diagrams are shown in Figure 3.1 and provide insight into how many photons of a given wavelength are striking the earth's surface during daylight hours.

Figure 3.1. Solar irradiance and inset of photon flux

107

In principle, macrocycles could be systematically tuned by modifying electronic properties and conjugation lengths. To further red-shift the absorption profile of the macrocycle in 2.31, a strategy was taken to tune the electronic density on the phenylacetylene arm 2.15. The inclusion of electron donating and withdrawing groups can create a push-pull system and potentially broaden of the absorption profile. Figure 3.2 depicts the macrocycle modifications aimed to red shift the absorption envelope.

Figure 3.2. A proposed design of the modified phenylacetylene arm (a) and its assembly with other building blocks to modified BODIPY-phenylacetylene macrocycle (b). EDG represents electron donating group whereas EWG represents electron withdrawing group

Before starting this proposed synthesis, an unsubstituted phenylacetylene arm (3.1) and its modified counterpart (3.4), (shown in Figure 3.3) were made and their absorption properties were studied, as a proof of concept.

108

Figure 3.3. Unsubstituted phenylacetylene 3.1 and its modified counterpart 3.4 with electron donating and accepting groups attached

UV-Vis data for both compounds 3.1 and 3.4 are shown in Figure 3.4. The absorption profile of the unsubstituted phenylacetylene 3.1 shows dominant absorption below 300 nm. However, attaching EDG and EWG red shifts the absorption profile significantly within the phenylacetylene arm 3.4 beyond 350 nm. Also, a broadened absorption (300-425 nm) is observed, which could be attributed to charge transfer between the EDG and EWG.

Figure 3.4. UV/Vis data for unsubstituted phenylacetylene arm 3.1 (dotted line) and modified phenylaceylene arm 3.4 (solid line)

109

DFT calculations were also run on the proposed modified BODIPY-phenylacetylene macrocycle and FMOs are shown in Figure 3.5. As it can be seen in Figure 3.5, the HOMO is localized on the donor part of the macrocycle whereas the LUMO stays on the acceptor unit. Based on electronic density splitting between these FMOs, it was hypothesized that upon absorption of a photon, electron density shifts from the lower part of the macrocycle (donor) to the upper side (acceptor) and eventually gets transferred to the BODIPY core.

Figure 3.5. DFT calculations on proposed modified BODIPY-phenylacetylene macrocycle (depicted by Figure 3.2b)

With DFT data in hand and experimental evidence to suggest that modification of the phenylacetylene arm (3.4) could permit a similar redshift in the absorption envelope of 2.31 modified BODIPY-phenylacetylene macrocycles were synthesized and studied using UV-Vis and fluorescence to determine if this is a viable approach (vide infra).

110

3.2. Synthesis
3.2.1. Synthesis of control phenylacetylene arms The first step in the synthesis was the preparation of the control phenylacetylene arm. Towards this end, an unsubstituted phenylacetylene arm 3.1 and its substituted derivative 3.4 were made. Synthesis of the unsubstituted phenylacetylene arm 3.1 was carried out by a Sonogashira cross-coupling between iodobenzene and ethynylbenzene (Scheme 3.1). Synthesis of 3.1 has been extensively studied in literature. A few articles have reported the synthesis of 3.1 via the Sonogashira cross-coupling reaction. However, they used a different protocol. In one such study, palladium nanoparticles supported on Smopex metal scavengers as catalysts have been used to synthesize 3.1.162 Gold and palladium combined for the Sonogashira coupling have also been used.163,164 Instead of following the literature methods, CuI and PhCl2(PPh3)2 catalysts together were used at RT and the product was obtained with 91% yield.

Scheme 3.1. Synthesis of unsubstituted phenylacetylene arm 3.1

Similar to 3.1, various methods have been reported in literature for the synthesis of 3.4. However, a general Sonogashira cross-coupling protocol was used to make a substituted derivative of 3.1. Synthesis of 3.4 was carried out according to Scheme 3.2. In the first step, 4iodoanisole was reacted with TMS-acetylene to yield 3.2, followed by deprotection to yield 3.3. Sonogashira cross-coupling reaction of 3.3 with 1-iodo-4-nitrobenzene yielded 3.4.

111

Scheme 3.2. Synthesis of substituted phenylacetylene arm 3.4

3.2.2. Attempted synthesis of new modified phenylacetylene arms: DA arms The first step towards the synthesis of the modified BODIPY-phenylacetylene macrocycle was the synthesis of the modified phenylacetylene arm. Because a red-shift was required in the macrocyclic part of the parent BODIPY-phenylacetylene macrocycle (2.31), the modification was required with phenylacetylene arms and the top BODIPY cap was kept the same. A cartoon showing this proposed design of the modified phenylacetylene arm is shown in Figure 3.6.

Figure 3.6. A proposed design of the modified phenylacetylene arm

To make the desired modified phenylacetylene arm (Figure 3.6), a donor (EDG) and acceptor (EWG) were required. The donor and acceptor units were designed in such a way that reacted them together could give the desired phenylacetylene arm. A proposed retrosynthesis is given in Scheme 3.3.
112

Scheme 3.3. A retrosynthetic scheme towards the synthesis of modified phenylacetylene arm 3.5 starting with donor 3.11 and acceptor 3.15

As shown in Scheme 3.3, the donor 3.11 and acceptor 3.15 were required to make the modified arm 3.5. The synthesis of donor 3.11 was carried out according to Scheme 3.4. The first step was the synthesis of 3.7, which has been reported in literature. One of the methods used 1iodo-2-methoxybenzene (2-iodoanisole) as a starting material.165 Fuming HNO3 was used to install a nitro-group (NO2) on 2-iodoanisole. To carry out this step, concentrated HNO3 was used instead of fuming HNO3 owing to its unavailability but this reaction failed to work. However, the same step was carried out using another literature method where they used oxidative iodination of deactivated arenes in concentrated H2SO4 with I2/NaIO4 or KI/NaIO4.166 This method was followed with the exception that KIO4 was used instead of NaIO4 and the product was obtained in 75% yield. The next step was the reduction of NO2 to NH2 to yield 3.8 and several literature methods for the reduction of the NO2 group were available. The Zn powder with glacial AcOH was used to carry out this step, but it failed to yield the desired product (3.8). Another method was pursued by using SnCl2.167 This method worked but the yield was low. The best yield for this reaction was obtained by using a patent method168 where they used Fe and NH4Cl as shown in Scheme 3.4. The next step in the synthesis of donor group 3.11 was to convert 3.8 to its triazine derivative 3.9 which was carried out via diazonium chemistry (yield of 73%). Compound 3.9 was then reacted with TMS-acetylene through a Sonogashira Cross-coupling reaction to yield 3.10 in good yield (70%). Reaction of TMS-protected triazine derivative 3.10 with CH3I yielded 3.11 in 89% yield.
113

Scheme 3.4. Synthesis of donor part of the modified phenylacetylene arm

With the donor group in hand, the next step was the synthesis of acceptor group 3.15. However, the synthesis of 3.15 was found to be challenging. The most suitable precursor was 5iodo-2-nitroaniline (3.12) which could be converted to 3.15 using known methodologies as shown in Scheme 3.5.

Scheme 3.5. Synthesis of acceptor unit 3.15 from 5-Iodo-2-nitroaniline (3.12)

The precursor 3.12 was commercially available from Synquest Labs, but was too expensive (USD. $2260 per 5 g) to purchase, particularly for something meant to be a low cast technology. A literature search revealed that only a few methods were available to make a bromo counterpart (3.16) of 3.12. Additionally, the literature methods were from patents.169,170 With no other option
114

available, patent methods were attempted to make 3.12 from 1-iodo-4-nitrobenzene (actual method used 1-bromo-4-nitrobenzene instead of 1-iodo-4-nitrobenzene). However, this reaction failed to yield the desired product (3.12). The reaction was then carried out on 1-bromo-4-nitrobenzene; again this step failed to yield 3.16. Some variations were made to the reagents (e.g. NH2OBn.HCl and NH2OH.HCl were used instead of NH2OMe.HCl) and reaction conditions (e.g. variations in temperature). All the efforts to synthesize it from different routes and reagents were in unsuccessful.

Scheme 3.6. Synthesis of compounds 3.12 and 3.16 starting with 1-iodo-4-nitrobenzene and 1-bromo-4nitrobenzene respectively

With the failure of reactions to prepare 3.12, the effort to make acceptor unit 3.15 was not pursued further. However, an alternative strategy to build the arm stepwise starting with the BODIPY cap was under taken (vide infra).

3.2.3. Stepwise synthesis of modified phenylacetylene arm starting with BODIPY-cap After an unsuccessful attempt to make modified phenylacetylene arm 3.5 from donor 3.11 and acceptor 3.15 groups, a new strategy starting with the top BODIPY cap and attaching an acceptor and then donor part (i.e. a gradual top-down strategy) to get to BODIPY-A-D premacrocycle was adopted. The cyclization of pre-macrocycle can yield the desired modified

115

BODIPY-phenylacetylene macrocycle. This strategy of stepwise synthesis starting with the BODIPY cap is depicted by a cartoon shown in Figure 3.7.

Figure 3.7. A stepwise strategy for the synthesis of modified BODIPY-phenylacetylene arm.

The same BODIPY cap (2.27) was used to start the synthesis as shown in Scheme 3.7. A new acceptor precursor 3.18 was selected for reacting with BODIPY 2.27. The synthesis of 3.18 was challenging too. However, the first step which involved the nitration of 1,3-diiodobenzene (Scheme 3.7) was previously reported.171,172 Both of the literature methods involve the reaction of fuming HNO3 with 1,3-diiodobenzene at 0 oC to yield the desired product (3.17). This procedure was followed to get 3.17 with the exception that instead of using fuming HNO3, concentrated HNO3 (70%) was used. It was noticed that the reaction does not proceed no matter how long it is stirred. However, this reaction worked when 1,3-diiodobenzene was heated to reflux with concentrated HNO3 for 12 hours and afforded the product 3.17 in nearly quantitative yield (96%). The next step was the synthesis of 3.18 which was achieved by reacting 3.17 with TMSacetylene via Sonogashira cross-coupling as shown in Scheme 3.1. This apparently simple looking reaction happened to be one of the most difficult reactions. Because there were two iodo-groups on the same molecule, reacting one group selectively over the other was problematic. The idea behind this proposed reaction was that the steric effect of the NO2 group might hinder the reaction at the ortho-position and hence prefer the reaction at the para-position.

116

Scheme 3.7. Synthesis of iodo-derivative with EWG (nitro) group (3.6)

The crude NMR showed impure product. The TLC showed two superimposing spots that were not separated by any solvent mixture. The two fractions were separated after extensive column chromatography using hexane as an eluent. One of the fractions was characterized as mono-substituted derivative (3.18) whereas the second fraction was a di-substituted derivative where both of the iodo-groups were replaced with TMS-acetylene. A 2D NMR (HMBC) spectrometry was used to confirm if the TMS-acetylene was at the para-position (para to NO2). The next step was the reaction of this new acceptor unit 3.18 with the previously made terminal alkyne BODIPY 2.27 using the Sonogashira cross-coupling reaction to yield 3.19 (Scheme 3.6). While the reaction was successful, the purification step was relatively difficult. Also, a few strange observations were made during this reaction; a) fluorescence was quenched b) the yield dropped unexpectedly, c) a dark red solid (insoluble to all available solvents) was obtained as a by-product. Generally speaking, a Sonogashira cross-coupling works in good to excellent yields. However, in the case of BODIPY, the Sonogashira reaction give yields somewhere from 50-70%. Surprisingly for this reaction to make 3.19, the yield dropped to 13%. As shown in Scheme 3.8, the next step was the deprotection of 3.19 to 3.20 which was obtained in 45% yield. Compound 3.20 was then reacted with donor unit 3.11 to yield TMSprotected macrocycle precursor 3.21. This reaction seemed to proceed but a pure product was never obtained. The cleanest fraction obtained after column chromatography showed the 1H NMR integrating for more than desired number of protons. Additionally, no clear results were obtained by mass spectrum (mass spectrum can be found in the appendices). Although the product was not pure, the next step was still attempted with hope to get the deprotected derivative 3.22. This was assumed based on the observation that TMS-protected BODIPY derivatives were almost always

117

greasy and that deprotection might yield a pure product. However, this reaction failed to yield 3.22. NMR studies failed to show any sign of product and further characterization was halted.

Scheme 3.8. Attempted synthesis of U-shape molecule 3.22 towards modified BODIPY-macrocycle

The failure of the above mentioned reactions were attributed to the decomposition of BODIPY during all preparation steps.

118

3.2.4. Modified phenylacetylene arms and its assembly with top BODIPY-cap To overcome this problem, a more stable BODIPY (tetramethyl BODIPY) 2.36 was reacted with acceptor group 3.18 to yield 3.23 (Scheme 3.9). A clean product was obtained by column chromatography. However, same observations were made as in the case of 3.19. The yield during this step was low (13%) and the fluorescence was quenched.

Scheme 3.9. Reaction of acceptor unit with tetramethyl-BODIPY to get 3.23

It was thought that the presence of NO2 at the ortho-position might be the reason for quenching of the fluorescence and also the lower yield. To study if that is the case, the same reaction was attempted with a donor group (methoxy derivative 3.9) rather than nitro derivative 3.18 to yield 3.17 (Scheme 3.8). Although the yield was better (21%) than that of 3.19 and 3.23, it was still low and unsatisfactory with seven more steps to get the desired product. As a result of low yielding reactions, this approach was abandoned at this stage.

119

Scheme 3.10. Synthesis of BODIPY cap with donor group (3.24)

3.2.5. Synthesis of new phenylacetylene arms and macrocycle: mono-donor arms As a result of the above mentioned failed attempts to make the modified phenylacetylene arms with donor and acceptor groups, the new strategy was to make a modified arm with only donor groups attached. This new arm is shown by a cartoon in Figure 3.8.

Figure 3.8. A cartoon showing a mono-donor phenylacetylene arm

This new arm 3.26 was made according to Scheme 3.11. The donor group 3.11 was reacted with previously made triazine alkyne 2.12 to yield 3.25 which was then reacted with iodomethane to yield the mono-donor phenylacetylene arm 3.26. Both of the steps gave products in good yields. Spectroscopic data for both of 3.25 and 3.26 is given in the appendices.

120

Scheme 3.11. Synthesis of mono-donor arm 3.26

The next step was to attach arm 3.26 to the BODIPY cap 2.27 and subsequently cyclize the ring as shown in Scheme 3.12. The first step involved the reaction of terminal alkyne BODIPY 2.27 with new phenylacetylene arm 3.26 to yield 3.27. Deprotection of 3.27 resulted in a U-shape molecule 3.28 which upon cyclization afforded the modified macrocycle 3.29. All of these compounds were fully characterized and the spectroscopic data is given in appendices.

121

Scheme 3.12. Synthesis of modified BODIPY-macrocycle 3.29

During the cyclization step, stoichiometric CuI was used and a yield of 23% was obtained which is an improvement from previous attempts for the cyclization step. Also, the bottom cap 2.17 was not used this time. This was the first time when the cyclization step was carried out without adding the bottom cap. However, the end result was the same. This adds to the evidence for the proposed mechanism during the cyclization step (Figure 2.16).

3.2.6. Synthesis of another mono-donor arm with donor group towards the BODIPY-cap Another phenylacetylene arm was made with mono-donor with donor groups on the upper side (towards BODIPY cap). Attaching this arm to the BODIPY cap could give a BODIPYphenylacetylene macrocycle with donor group on the top side of the macrocycle. The motivation for this structure was to study how the process of energy transfer differs in both cases (one where the donor group is on the lower side of the macrocycle and the other where it is on the upper side). A cartoon representing this kind of modified phenylacetylene arm is shown in Figure 3.9.

Figure 3.9. A cartoon representing a mono-donor phenylacetylene are with donor group on top

To make this new mono-donor arm (shown in Figure 3.9), a new donor group (3.32) made from 5-iodo-2-methoxyaniline in three steps (as shown in Scheme 3.13) was required. 5-iodo-2methoxyaniline was obtained from Alfa Aesar and converted to triazine derivative 3.30 via diazonium chemistry which was further reacted with TMS-acetylene through Sonogashira crosscoupling to yield 3.31. Finally, the deprotection of 3.31 with KF yielded 3.32. All of the steps gave high yields. The spectroscopic data is shown in appendices.

Scheme 3.13. Synthesis of alkyne derivative 3.32

To make the desired arm 3.34, methoxy alkyne derivative 3.32 was reacted with 2.13 to yield 3.33. The next step was the conversion of triazine to iodo group (3.34) which was achieved by reacting 3.33 with iodomethane. The synthetic steps to make 3.34 are depicted in Scheme 3.14.

Scheme 3.14. Synthesis of compound 3.34

Both of the derivatives, 3.33 and 3.34, were made in excellent yields (92% and 93% respectively). They were fully characterized by spectroscopic techniques and the data is given in the appendices. The modified phenylacetylene arm 3.34 was then used to build the second modified BODIPY-phenylacetylene macrocycle 3.37 using a reaction Scheme 3.15. All of these steps were similar to the one described in Scheme 3.12. All of the intermediates as well as the product (3.37) were fully characterized and the spectroscopic data is given in the appendices.

Scheme 3.15. Synthesis of modified BODIOY-macrocycle 3.37

3.2.6. Synthesis of new phenyl acetylene arms and macrocycle: bis-donor arms After the successful synthesis of two modified BODIPY-phenylacetylene macrocycles (one with mono-donor groups on the lower side of the macrocycle (3.29) and the other with monodonor on the upper side of the macrocycle (3.36)), another macrocycle with bis-donor groups was made. For this purpose, a modified phenylacetylene arm with bis-donor groups was needed. A cartoon showing the new arm with bis-donor groups is shown in Figure 3.10.

Figure 3.10. A design of a modified phenylacetylene arm with bis-donor group

The synthesis of the bis-donor arm is shown in Scheme 3.16. Both of the precursors (3.32 and 3.11) have been previously made and reacting them using Sonogashira cross-coupling protocol yielded 3.38 which was converted to the desired arm (3.39) by heating with iodomethane.

Scheme 3.16. Synthesis of compound 3.39

The next step was the reaction of bis-donor arm 3.39 and BODIPY 2.27 to yield new modified BODIPY-phenylacetylene macrocycle 3.42 as according to Scheme 3.17. The first step was to make the TMS-protected U-shape molecule 3.40 which upon deprotection yielded a pre8macrocycle molecule 3.41. Cyclization of 3.41 using a previously explored protocol yielded the desired product 3.42. Each step was fully characterized and the spectroscopic data for each step is given in the appendices.

3.3. Results and Discussion
3.3.1. Photophysical properties of modified BODIPY-phenylacetylene macrocycle 3.29 The absorption data of compound 3.29 was collected in a DCM solution (Figure 3.16). The macrocyclic part of compound 3.29 absorbs at 296 nm along with a sharp absorption at 367 nm whereas the BODIPY portion exhibits a typical BODIPY absorption at 511 nm with a shoulder at 487 nm. When 3.29 is excited at 296 nm, the emission is observed from BODIPY core at 528 nm.

Figure 3.16. UV-Vis spectra of 3.29 & 2.31; fluorescence emission spectrum from 3.29

The TD-DFT (6-31G*) was used to assign transitions in FMOs of compound 3.29. Table 3.1 summarizes these transitions. In the table, f represents the oscillator strength and is a relative
133

value for intensity of the absorption. Orbital contributions (Cont. (%)) represents the percent contribution of a particular set of orbitals involved in the transition. The highest value of oscillator strength represents the transition at 296 nm which is in accordance with the absorption profile and is assigned to the HOMO-1 to LUMO+1 transition. The FMOs of compound 3.29 are shown in Figure 3.17. The second highest absorption is due to the BODIPY core which is assigned to the HOMO-2 to LUMO transition.

3.3.4. Emission profile of 3.29 at variable concentrations All of the modified macrocycles (3.29, 3.37 and 3.42) show energy transfer from the macrocycle to the BODIPY core as can be seen upon excitation of the macrocyclic portion of the compound, and an emission characteristic of BODIPY. To eliminate co-concentration dependence as a variable on the emission profile, the emission data at different concentrations of 3.29 was collected. It was observed that intensity of the emission is directly proportional to the concentration (Figure 3.22) suggesting no significant concentration behavior. In Figure 3.22, a blue solid line represents the absorption profile of modified BODIPY-macrocycle 3.29 and the dotted lines represents the emission data for different concentrations of 3.29 (with the concentrations decreasing from 1 to 4). This trend was consistent with the other modified macrocycles (3.37 and 3.42) as well.

Figure 3.22. Absorption spectrum (solid blue line) and emission spectra (dotted lines) of 3.29 with decreasing concentrations from 1-4

142

3.4. Summary and Conclusion
In this chapter an attempt was made to modify the previously made BODIPYphenylacetylene macrocycle 2.31 in which the energy transfer is observed from macrocycle to the BODIPY core. The macrocycle in 2.31 absorbs around 300 nm and to red-shift the absorption envelop of the macrocycle part, it was modified by attaching donor (although acceptor groups were also explored) on the phenylacetylene arms. An attempt to implement a push-pull strategy between the donor and acceptor groups was unsuccessful owing to synthetic difficulties. However, the modified macrocycles were successfully made by substituting mono-donor groups (on one side of the macrocycle) and bis-donor groups (on both sides of the macrocycle). The absorption studies show that the modified BODIPY-phenylacetylene macrocycle with bis-donor group (3.42) displayed a more red-shifted absorption profile as compared to those with mono-donor groups (3.29 and 3.37). The absorption envelope in 3.42 was extended up to 380 nm which indicates a bathochromic shift of about 50 nm as compared to the parent BODIPYphenylacetylene macrocycle 2.31. TD-DFT calculations were performed to assign the dominant transitions in the FMOs of the modified macrocycles. The fluorescence studies showed the energy transfer from the macrocycle to the BODIPY upon excitation of the macrocyclic part. However, a curious concentration dependent emission was observed for the family of methoxy-substituted macrocycles (3.29, 3.37 & 3.42) from 380-450 nm which increases with decreasing the concentration of the modified BODIPY-phenylacetylene macrocycle.

143

3.5. Experimental
3.5.1. General considerations Unless stated otherwise, all reagents were purchased from Sigma Aldrich except PdCl2 (Pressure Chemical Co., Pittsburg, PA). PdCl2 was reacted with PPh3 to make catalyst PdCl2(PPh3)2. Purification by column chromatography was carried out using silica (Silicycle: ultrapure flash silica). Analytical thin-layer chromatography was performed on aluminum-backed sheets precoated with silica 60 F254 adsorbent (0.25 mm thick; Silicycle) and visualized under UV light. Routine 1H, 13C {1H}, 11B {1H} and 19F NMR spectra were recorded at 400, 100, 128 and 376 MHz, respectively, on a Bruker AV 400 instrument at ambient temperature. Chemical shifts () are reported in parts per million (ppm) from low to high field and referenced to a residual nondeuterated solvent (CHCl3) for 1H and 13C nuclei and BF3·OEt2 (11B nucleus;  = 0 ppm) C6F6 (19F nucleus;  = 0 ppm). Standard abbreviations indicating multiplicity are used as follows: s = singlet; d = doublet; q = quartet and br = broad. High resolution mass spectrometry (HRMS) results were obtained from Queens University, Kingston Ontario. Electron impact (EI) mass spectrometry and Electrospray ionization (ESI) techniques were used for the ionization; time of flight (TOF) was used for analysis. UV-Vis data was taken using Cary Series UV-Vis-NIR Spectrophotometer from Agilent Technologies and dichloromethane (having the onset peak at 230 nm) was used as the solvent. The GAUSSIAN 09 computational package154 was used to perform ground-state geometry optimization calculations employing Becke's three-parameter hybrid exchange functional and the Lee­Yang-Parr non-local correlation functional B3LYP155-157 and 6-31G basis set was used for all the atoms. Time-dependent density functional theory calculations were also performed using this methodology, and the first 200 singlet excited states were calculated. Calculations by the first-principles method were used to obtain accurate excitation energies and oscillator strengths. We modelled the solvent with the polarizable continuum model using DCM as the solvent.158

144

3.5.2. Synthesis The synthetic details of each compound are given below. General synthetic steps have been used unless otherwise mentioned in the procedure.

1,2-diphenylethyne (3.1). Iodobenzene (2.40 g, 11.75 mmol) was dissolved in 104 mL of THF:NEt3 (1:1) and sparged with nitrogen for 15 min. PdCl2(PPh3)2 (0.344 g, 0.490 mmol) and CuI (0.186 g, 0.979 mmol) were added followed by the addition of phenylacetylene (1.0 g, 9.79 mmol). The resulting mixture was stirred overnight at RT. The solution was filtered, the solvent removed from the filtrate, dissolved in EtOAc, and washed with an NH4Cl solution. The extract was then washed with brine, dried over MgSO4, and filtered. The solvent was evaporated in vacuo, and the residue was chromatographed using pure hexanes (Rf = 0.31) to yield product 3.1 as a light yellow crystalline solid (1.59 g, 91%). 1H NMR (CDCl3, 400 MHz):  = 7.66-7.64 (m, 2 H), 7.457.42 (m, 3H). 13C NMR (CDCl3, 100 MHz):  = 131.72, 128.46, 128.36, 123.40, 89.54.

((4-methoxyphenyl)ethynyl)trimethylsilane (3.2). Iodoanisole (1.0 g) was dissolved in 50 mL of THF:NEt3 (1:1) and sparged with nitrogen for 15 min. PdCl2(PPh3)2 (0.150 g, 0.214 mmol) and CuI (0.081 g, 0.427 mmol) were added as catalysts followed by the addition TMS-acetylene (0.504 g, 5.127 mmol). The resulting mixture was stirred overnight at RT. The solution was then filtered,
145

the solvent removed from the filtrate, dissolved in EtOAc, and washed with an NH4Cl solution. The extract was then washed with brine, dried over MgSO4, and filtered. The solvent was evaporated in vacuo, and the residue was chromatographed using Hex:EtOAc (14:1, Rf = 0.38) to yield the pure product (3.2). 1H NMR (CDCl3, 400 MHz):  = 7.40 (d, J = 8.0 Hz, 2H), 6.80 (d, J = 8.0 Hz, 2H), 3.76 (s, 3H), 0.26 (s, 9H).

1-ethynyl-4-methoxybenzene (3.3). Protected alkyne 3.2 (1 g, 4.89 mmol) was dissolved in 50 mL of THF:MeOH (1:1) and reacted with excess KF (0.85 g, 3 eq). The reaction was left to stir overnight at RT. The reaction mixture was poured into water and extracted with EtOAc. The organic layer was dried over MgSO4, filtered, and the solvent evaporated under reduce pressure to yield the pure product (3.3) in good yield (0.51 g, 79%). 1H NMR (CDCl3, 400 MHz):  = 7.43 (d, J = 8.0 Hz, 2H), 6.84 (d, J = 8.0, 2H), 3.81 (s, 3H), 3.00 (s, 1H).

1-methoxy-4-((4-nitrophenyl)ethynyl)benzene (3.4). 1-iodo-4-nitrobenzene (0.678 g, 2.72 mmol) was dissolved in 24 mL of THF:NEt3 (1:1) and sparged with nitrogen for 15 minutes. PdCl2(PPh3)2 (0.08 g, 0.113 mmol) and CuI (0.043 g, 0.227) were added as catalysts followed by the addition of terminal alkyne derivative 3.3 (0.30 g, 2.27 mmol). The mixture was stirred overnight at RT. The solution was filtered, the solvent removed from the filtrate, dissolved in

EtOAc, and washed with an NH4Cl solution. The extract was then washed with brine, dried over MgSO4, and filtered. Attempted synthesis of 5-iodo-2-nitroaniline (3.12). The mixture of t-BuOK (5.63, 5 eq) and CuCl (0.1 g, 0.1 eq) ethylene glycol dimethylether (1,2-dimethoxyethane) (18.58 ml) is prepared at 0 oC under a nitrogen atmosphere. The 1-iodo-4-nitrobenzene (2.5 g, 1 eq) and NH2OBn. HCl (2.56, 1.6 eq) was added in DMF (10 ml). To this the above DME solution mixture was added drop wise over 30 minutes at 0 oC. After completion of the addition, the cooling bath was removed and the contents stirred at RT for 6h. The completion of the reaction was monitored by TLC. The yellow coloured solid product was obtained after thorough purification with ice water. The solid was filtered and dried over vacuum. Purification was attempted by flash chromatography (using various solvent mixtures), a pure compound was never obtained. The starting material was recovered in high yield (~80%) which means the reaction never took place.

The solvent was evaporated in vacuo, and the residue was chromatographed using Hex:DCM (1:2, Rf = 0.26) to give the product as a dark red solid (21 mg, 23%). 1H NMR CDCl3, 400 MHz):  = 8.10 (s, 2H), 8.05 (d, J = 2.0 Hz, 2H), 7.70 (s, 2H), 7.36 (dd, J = 2.0, 8.5 Hz, 2H), 7.31 (dd, J = 2.0, 8.5 Hz, 2H), 7.03 (d, J = 2.0 Hz, 2H), 6.93 (d, J = 4.0 Hz, 2H), 6.82 (d, J = 8.5 Hz, 2H), 6.76 (d, J = 8.5 Hz, 2H), 6.48 (d, J = 4.0 Hz, 2H), 3.92 (s, 6H), 3.91 (s, 6H), 1.44 (s, 9H). 13C NMR (CDCl3, 100 MHz):  = 159.51, 158.84, 152.66, 144.62, 141.53, 138.79, 135.20, 131.55, 131.09, 129.21, 128.95, 128.92, 128.91, 123.89, 118.50, 116.16, 116.00, 112.36, 112.10, 110.67, 110.31, 91.94, 90.40, 88.16, 88.13, 79.74, 78.86, 56.00, 55.92, 34.96, 31.06.

Chapter 4: Functionalizing BODIPY Dyes for DSSC Applications
4.1. Introduction
Ultimately, this research sought to develop a dye suitable for light-harvesting applications. To this end, a BODIPY-phenylacetylene macrocycle could be appended with a donor (e.g. triphenylamine) and accepting group (e.g. cyanoacetic acid groups derived from aldehydes), allowing it to effectively integrate into the DSSC. The donor groups could be added to the aforementioned family of dyes in a variety of ways, and two strategies are depicted in Figure 4.1.

Figure 4.1. Proposed design for the attachment of donor groups to the BODIPY-phenylacetylene macrocycle; a) donor (D) and acceptor (A) groups are attached to BODIPY, b) D and A attached to macrocycle

In the pursuit of these modified dyes, a number of small benchmark compounds were prepared. As the synthesis of a BODIPY-phenylacetylene macrocyclic dye was not realized, the information collated here will be of value for future DSSC dye design, as it builds on the Koivisto group's previous work towards dye design (Figure 4.2).90 It should also be noted that this work was conducted concurrently to macrocycle synthesis, and as such it was performed in anticipation of integrating these building blocks into the previously discussed macrocyclic scaffolds.

171

Figure 4.2. Using BODIPY as a -spacer with donor and acceptor groups for DSSC applications

This chapter describes supplementary syntheses, physicochemical characterization of compounds indirectly related to the objectives of this thesis. This chapter is divided into three sections. Section 4.2.1 describes the attempts to make DSSC dyes using BODIPYs as -spacers. These attempts were not performed on BODIPY-macrocycle 2.31 but were explored using simpler -spacers. Section 4.2.2 describes the synthesis of meso-substituted BODIPYs by putting electron donating groups on both the methylated-BODIPY (tetra-methyl BODIPY) and unsubstituted BODIPY. Additionally, the physicochemical properties of meso-substituted BODIPYS were studied and are discussed in 4.3 (results and discussion). In addition, section 4.2.3 describes electrophilic addition reactions in the presence of BODIPY alkyne species.

172

4.2. Synthesis
4.2.1. Synthesis of BODIPY dyes with donor and anchor groups A retrosynthetic scheme for a BODIPY-phenylacetylene macrocycle DSSC dye is proposed in Scheme 4.1. According to this approach, the BODIPY group could be formylated, then iodinated and subsequent cross-coupling reaction with a donor group to yield a derivative where finally a Knoevenagel condensation would yield the desired product. Scheme 4.1 shows cartoon of the target a) and the proposed dye retrosynthesis.

Scheme 4.1. Retrosynthesis approach for BODIPY-macrocycle Dye

To realize this target, one of the first steps towards this D--A dye motif is the synthesis of a triphenylamine (TPA) donor group. TPA was chosen owing to its redox robustness and the Koivisto group's extensive experience with this group. Synthesis of
173

TPA was carried out

according to Scheme 4.2. Bismethoxy-TPA 4.1 was made via an Ullmann condensation reaction

followed by electrophilic aromatic substitution (bromination) to yield 4.2. Lithiation of 4.2 and quenching with 2-isopropoxy-4,4,5,5-tetramethyl-1,3,2-dioxaborolane gave boronic ester 4.3 which serves as a suitable precursor (to be used later) for a Suzuki coupling reaction.

Scheme 4.2. Synthesis of donor chromophore 4.3

To test this retrosynthetic methodology (Scheme 4.1), before reacting on the BODIPYphenylacetylene macrocycle -spacer, a trial was performed using a simpler BODIPY (Scheme 4.3).90 For this purpose, pentamethyl BODIPY 4.4 was made from acetyl chloride and iodinated to yield 4.5 as shown in Scheme 4.5. The reaction of iodo-BODIPY 4.5 with 4.3 through a Suzuki cross-coupling reaction yielded 4.6. The next two steps involve the formylation of 4.6 to yield 4.7 and the addition of a cyanoacetic acid anchor group using a Knoevenagel condensation reaction to give the DA dye motif 4.8.

174

Scheme 4.3. Synthesis of DSSC dye 4.8 with a with D--A dye motif

Although dye 4.8 was already reported in literature,90 it was resynthesized as a benchmark compound for this thesis. This exercise also aided in becoming familiar with the challenges involved in the synthesis of BODIPY dyes. In anticipation of a U-shape Sonogashira coupling (see section 2.2.4), building block 2.9 was converted into 4.10 (Scheme 4.4). The first step was the substitution of one side of the BODIPY with an aldehyde via a Vilsmeier-Haack reaction to yield 4.9. The electrophilic addition reaction was done to get the iodo derivative of 4.10. Iodo-BODIPY derivative 4.10 was then reacted with 4.3 via a Suzuki cross-coupling reaction to incorporate the donor group on BODIPY
175

(4.11) but this reaction failed to yield the desired product. It was assumed that this failure could be due to competitive reactions with two other iodo groups on the phenyl ring located at the mesoposition of BODIPY 4.10.

Scheme 4.4. Synthesis of aldehyde derivative 4.10 as a precursor to D--A dye motif

In order to minimize wasting important building blocks, the above failed reaction was further investigated using a simple phenyl ring at the meso-position (4.12). BODIPY 4.12 was synthesized from benzaldehyde and 2,4-dimethyl-1H-pyrrole. BODIPY 4.12 was then formylated to 4.13 followed by iodination to yield 4.14. Iodo- BODIPY 4.14 was then reacted with donor unit 4.3 to yield 4.15. Again, the Suzuki cross-coupling reaction failed to yield 4.15. This result was discouraging as it was counter to our previous iodide hypothesis. At this point, this particular protocol was abandoned owing to concurrent breakthroughs in macrocycle synthesis (see section 2.2.6 and 2.2.10).

176

Scheme 4.5. Synthesis of BODIPY-aldehyde with phenyl group at meso-position (4.15)

Another possible strategy is to perform the iodination and the Suzuki cross-coupling reaction before the formyl group is appended to BODIPY. Although, this route was never pursued, a proposed synthetic scheme (similar to retrosynthesis) for the synthesis of DSSC dye is given in Scheme 4.6.

4.2.2. Substitution at the meso-position of BODIPY In anticipation of dye design (Figure 4.1), the effect of meso-substitution on BODIPY was investigated. Electron donating groups were substituted at the meso-position with the hopes of redshifting the absorption profile of the macrocycle. Meso-substituted BODIPYs were categorized into two sets. One set of BODIPYs was made with unsubstituted pyrrole and is referred to as unsubstituted-BODIPY (although it is substituted at the meso-position), whereas the other set was made with 2,4-dimethyl-1H-pyrrole to give the methylated-BODIPY (Figure 4.3). A cartoon with

BODIPYs (Figure 4.3) were made using appropriate benzaldehydes, most of which were commercially available except 4-(bis(4-methoxyphenyl)amino)benzaldehyde (4.16) which was synthesized according to Scheme 4.7.

4.2.3. Iodination and Formylation on the 2,6-positions of meso-substituted BODIPYs One serious concern was whether the phenylacetylene portion of the macrocycle would be able to tolerate electrophilic addition reactions to the BODIPY core; a critical necessity in our long term synthetic methodology. Figure 4.4, shows a generic meso-substituted BODIPY with 2,6positions available for the iodination and/or formylation reaction. Another important

consideration was whether unsubstituted-BODIPY cores would be able to effectively undergo mono-iodination. To this end, trial reactions were done as shown in Scheme 4.11.

Figure 4.4. A cartoon showing iodination and formylation reactions at the 2,6-postions of a mesosubstituted BODIPY 181

With the knowledge that iodination reactions work fairly well on methylated-BODIPY (e.g. 4.3 and 4.12), it was desirable to check if the iodination reaction works the same way on unsubstituted-BODIPY. Pleasingly, an iodination reaction was carried out successfully on 4.20 using NIS (Scheme 4.11) to yield its iodo derivative 4.27, and minimal di-iodo product was observed.

Scheme 4.10. Iodination of BODIPY 4.20 to yield the iodo-derivative 4.27

The iodination and formylation reactions were also attempted using a TMS-protected alkyne BODIPY 2.35 to check its tolerance to these reaction conditions. Both the iodination and formylation reactions on 2.35 worked fairly well and are shown in Scheme 4.12. Both the formylation and iodination reactions can be done interchangeably. The iodination and formylation reactions were studied on different BODIPYs ranging from less bulky groups at the meso-positon (4.4, 4.12 and 4.17) to more sterically hindered groups (2.9, 4.9). In each case, the result was the same i.e. good yields during all these reactions, regardless of how bulky the group is at the mesoposition; however, if the iodination is done before the Vilsmeier-Haack formylation, the overall yield drops.

a

4.3.1. Absorption behaviour of meso-substituted BODIPYs The UV-Vis data of unsubstituted-BODIPYs (Figure 4.5) shows that the absorption is not significantly red-shifted by changing the aryl groups (EDG) at the meso-position compared to the parent BODIPY 4.17. However, when a redox active species is present at the meso-position a charge transfer band results (4.22 from 525-625 nm). The charge transfer band is from the excited TPA unit which likely donates electron density to excited BODIPY core.

186

Figure 4.5. UV-Vis absorption data for unsubstituted BODIPYs

A similar trend was observed in the case of methylated BODIPYs. Only modest changes where observed and a slight red-shift in absorption occurred by attaching different EDGs as compared to parent BODIPY 4.4 (Figure 4.6). One intriguing difference however, was that now charge transfer band was not prominent in 4.26. One hypothesis for this observation could be due to the fact that the methyl-substitution maintains a rigorous orthogonality between the BODIPY and TPA -systems, effectively prohibiting significant charge transfer.

187

Figure 4.6. UV-Vis absorption data for methylated BODIPYs

4.3.2. Theoretical investigation (DFT/TD-DFT) of meso-substituted BODIPYs DFT calculations for both families of building blocks have been summarized in Figures 4.7 and 4.8. Lending support to the previous hypothesis (vide supra), when comparing the optimized geometries of 4.22 and 4.26, the methylated BODIPY has the TPA unit strictly orthogonal, while the non-methylated derivative 4.22 has the plane canted by about 70o. This seems to have a significant impact on the optical properties as the HOMOs reside on the BODIPY cores whereas in the case of LUMOs, the electron density is localized on the meso-substituted aryl groups. This trend is found with all BODIPY derivatives except 4.22 and 4.26 which have HOMOs lying on the TPA unit and LUMO at the meso-position. This supports the rationale for the observed charge transfer as shown in Figure 4.5. The end result is that the new HOMO is on the TPA unit and the LUMO is on the meso-position of BODIPY.

188

Figure 4.7. TD-DFT calculations on unsubstituted BODIPYs (3-21G). Dominant optical transitions have been highlighted with a dashed line, and the secondary contribution highlighted with a solid line.

189

The TD-DFT calculations were used to assign the dominant transitions which are shown in Table 4.1 and Table 4.2. The dominant transitions (denoted by dotted arrows) in all the cases were from HOMO to LUMO. The secondary transitions (denoted by solid arrows) in FMOs are also shown. In either case the transitions involve the shift of electron density from the BODIPY core to the meso-position.

190

Figure 4.8. TD-DFT calculations on methylated-BODIPYs (3-21G). Dominant optical transitions have been highlighted with a dashed line, and the secondary contribution highlighted with a solid line.

191

4.3.3. Electrochemical behaviour of meso-substituted BODIPYs Cyclic voltammetry (CV) is used as an electroanalytical technique to study redox active behaviour of analytes. It is a quantitative and qualitative technique, which can be used to determine the formal potential of a half-reaction or the stability of a redox species. A reversible oxidation potential allows for the determination of the HOMO energy and a reversible reduction allows for the determination of the LUMO energy; however, these are solvent and supporting electrolyte dependent approximations. The HOMO-LUMO gap can be calculated by taking the difference between the HOMO and LUMO potentials. The cyclic voltammetry experiment is carried out by applying a control potential to the analyte. The experiment involve three electrodes connected to a potentiostat and inserted into a solution consists of a supporting electrolyte and the analyte (the solvent must have an electrochemical window larger than the potential region which the sample is run through). A cyclic voltammogram (potential-current curve) is generated when a potentiostat varies the potential of the working electrode with respect to the reference electrode.173,174 The counter-electrode is required to complete the circuit i.e. during the working electrode's anodic sweep (oxidative mode), the counter-electrode acts as a cathode (reductive mode) and vice versa.174 Cyclic voltammetry (CV) data (summarized in Table 4.1 and Table 4.2) show a quasireversible oxidation for unsubstituted BODIPYs which is due to BODIPY oxidation occurring close to the solvent decomposition edge of the voltammogram (Figure 4.9). In these derivatives, there is also an observable quasi-reversible reduction. This reduction is not typically observed in our methylated BODIPY derivatives; however, the LUMO of unsubstituted BODIPYs lies within the solvent window and is reduced before the solvent (Figure 4.9). The two dotted lines in Figure 4.9 represent the DCM solvent stability window in our electrochemical studies.

192

Figure 4.9. HOMO-LUMO energy gap for methylated BODIPY and unsubstituted BODIPY having donor group (R) at the meso-position. The two dotted lines represent the solvent window

In the case of more electron-rich methylated-BODIPYs, HOMOs lie within the solvent window and these derivatives are reversibly oxidized (Figure 4.9). Additionally, methylatedBODIPYs possess a fully reversible oxidation owing to substitution at their 1,3,5 and 7 positions which prevents decomposition (Table 4.1 and Table 4.2). A representative voltammogram for meso-substituted BODIPYs is shown in Figure 4.10 (see appendices for detailed CV data). The oxidation potential (E1/2) is determined by calculating the average of the anodic (Ea) and cathodic (Ep) potentials.

193

Figure 4.10. A reversible cyclic voltammogram (CV) of 4.12; Ea represents the anodic peak, Ec represents cathodic peak and E/12 represents formal redox potential (oxidation potential).

The CV data also shows that it is slightly harder to reduce the unsubstituted BODIPYs when strong EDGs are attached (Table 4.1). This increase in the reduction potential is attributed to the destabilization of the LUMO. Unsurprisingly, TPA substituted BODIPYs (i.e., 4.26) observes more than one oxidation events owing to the TPA group that is readily oxidized; however, it is interesting to note that in both the methylated and non-methylated derivatives the trimethoxysubstituted derivative is also redox active and while it may not demonstrate any unique behaviour optically, it could be leveraged as a redox-active donor. Finally, consistent with the DFT and UVVis data, it is also observed that the HOMOs of methylated-BODIPYs are only modestly perturbed to higher energy as compared to their unsubstituted counterparts.

194

4.4. Summary and conclusion
In this chapter, based on previously reported dye design strategy, a series of model reactions were explored towards making a D--A dye motif. During these attempted reactions, it was observed that the Suzuki cross-coupling reactions pose a challenge in the presence of a BODIPY aldehydes. Avoiding Suzuki cross-coupling reactions on formylated BODIPYs, the best strategy could be to iodinate a BODIPY first, attach a donor group to it through Suzuki crosscoupling, and then formylate it via a Vilsmeier-Haack formylation reaction. A number of unsubstituted-BODIPYs and methylated BODIPYs having different donor groups at the meso-position were synthesized and studied for their physicochemical properties. The HOMOs lie on the BODIPY core in most of the BODIPYs except two which have TPA donor groups. In the BODIPYs with TPA donor groups at the meso-position have HOMOs which reside on the TPA unit and a new HOMO is created. However, similar to the rest of the BODIPYs, the LUMOs resides on the meso-position. It was also observed that LUMO energy increases with the increased electronic density at the meso-position. Additionally, unsubstituted BODIPYs decompose upon oxidation resulting in quasi-reversible oxidations whereas, the methylated BODIPYs are not prone to decomposition and witness a reversible oxidation. Formylation and iodination reactions were studied at 2,6-positions of various BODIPYs. It was concluded that both reactions work reasonably well regardless of the presence of bulky groups on the BODIPY, and should not pose a significant challenge in dye integration moving forward. However, the formylation reaction can be done more selectively as compared to iodation which is prone to competition reactions when both the 2 and 6-positions are available on BODIPY. If the iodination reaction is required before the Vilsmeier-Haack formylation reaction, keeping the iodinating agent (NIS) as a limiting reagent can help increase the yield during the iodination reaction.

195

4.5. Experimental
4.5.1. General considerations Unless stated otherwise, all reagents were purchased from Sigma Aldrich except PdCl2 (Pressure Chemical Co., Pittsburg, PA). PdCl2 was reacted with PPh3 to make catalyst PdCl2(PPh3)2. Purification by column chromatography was carried out using silica (Silicycle: ultrapure flash silica). Analytical thin-layer chromatography was performed on aluminum-backed sheets precoated with silica 60 F254 adsorbent (0.25 mm thick; Silicycle) and visualized under UV light. Routine 1H, 13C{1H}, 11B{1H} and 19F NMR spectra were recorded at 400, 100, 128 and 376 MHz, respectively, on a Bruker AV 400 instrument at ambient temperature. Chemical shifts () are reported in parts per million (ppm) from low to high field and referenced to a residual nondeuterated solvent (CHCl3) for 1H and 13C nuclei and BF3·OEt2 (11B nucleus;  = 0 ppm) C6F6 (19F nucleus;  = 0 ppm). Standard abbreviations indicating multiplicity are used as follows: s = singlet; d = doublet; q = quartet and br = broad. High resolution mass spectrometry (HRMS) results were obtained from Queens University, Kingston Ontario. Electron impact (EI) mass spectrometry and Electrospray ionization (ESI) techniques were used for the ionization; time of flight (TOF) was used for analysis. UV-Vis data was taken using Cary Series UV-Vis-NIR Spectrophotometer from Agilent Technologies and dichloromethane (having the onset peak at 230 nm) was used as the solvent. The GAUSSIAN 09 computational package154 was used to perform ground-state geometry optimization calculations employing Becke's three-parameter hybrid exchange functional and the Lee­Yang-Parr non-local correlation functional B3LYP155-157 and 6-31G basis set was used for all the atoms. Time-dependent density functional theory calculations were also performed using this methodology, and the first 200 singlet excited states were calculated. Calculations by the first-principles method were used to obtain accurate excitation energies and oscillator strengths. We modelled the solvent with the polarizable continuum model using DCM as the solvent.158

4.5.2. Synthesis The synthetic details for each compound are given below. General synthetic steps have been used unless otherwise mentioned in the procedure.

4-methoxy-N-(4-methoxyphenyl)-N-phenylaniline (4.1).Under an atmosphere of N2, piodoanisole (6.35 g, 27 mmol), aniline (1.0 mL, 11 mmol) and 1,10-phenanthroline (0.39 g, 2.2 mmol) were solubilized in 20 mL of toluene. The solution was heated to 100 oC followed by the addition of CuCl (0.22 g, 2.2 mmol) and KOH (4.9 g, 87 mmol). The resulting mixture was refluxed overnight. After being cooled to room temperature, 3 mL of glacial AcOH and 15 mL of toluene were added. The mixture was washed with H2O once and extracted with toluene. The combined organic layers were dried over MgSO4, filtered and volatiles were removed in vacuo. The crude product was purified via column chromatography using Hex:EtOAc (9:1, Rf = 0.6) to yield the product as a yellow solid (3.80 g, 46%). NMR data agreed with the literature values. 1H NMR (CDCl3, 400 MHz):  = 7.17 (t, J = 8.0 Hz, 2H), 7.05 (m, 4H), 6.94 (d, J = 8.0 Hz, 2H), 6.87 (t, J = 8.0 Hz, 1H), 6.84-6.80 (m, 4H), 3.80 (s, 6H).

4-bromo-N,N-bis(4-methoxyphenyl)aniline (4.2). Under an atmosphere of N2, 4-methoxy-N-(4methoxyphenyl)-N-phenylaniline 4.1 (1.98 g, 6.48 mmol) was dissolved in 46 mL of THF:EtOAc (26:20) and N-bromosuccinimide (1.38 g, 7.78 mmol) was added in one portion. The flask was covered with Al foil and the mixture was stirred overnight. Volatiles were removed in vacuo and the crude was purified via column chromatography using Hex:EtOAc (9:1, Rf = 0.5) as the eluent affording the desired product as a yellowish solid (2.46 g, 99%). 4-methoxy-N-(4-methoxyphenyl)-N-(4-(4,4,5,5-tetramethyl-1,3,2-dioxaborolan-2yl)phenyl)aniline (4.3). Under an atmosphere of N2, 4-bromo-N,N-bis(4-methoxyphenyl)aniline 4.2 (2.46 g, 6.40 mmol) was dissolved in 23 mL of dry THF and the solution was cooled to -78

5,5-difluoro-1,3,7,9,10-pentamethyl-5H-44,54-dipyrrolo[1,2-c:2',1'-f][1,3,2]diazaborinine (4.4). In a 250 Schlenk flask, under an atmosphere of N2, acetyl chloride (3.23 g, 41.1 mmol) was added dropwise at room temperature over 30 minutes to a solution of pyrrole (1.70 g, 17.9 mmol) in 10 mL of dry DCM. The mixture was refluxed for 1 hour and after cooling to room temperature, 50 mL of hexanes was added to the mixture. Volatiles were removed in vacuo and 120 mL of dry DCM were added. NEt3 (7.0 mL) was added and the mixture was stirred for 10 min. BF3.OEt2 (9.4 mL) was added dropwise and stirred for 1 hour at RT. The mixture was washed with NaHCO3 and the organic layer was dried over MgSO4, filtered and concentrated in vacuo. The oily residue was chromatographed using Hex:DCM (1:1, Rf = 0.62). The resulting solid was recrystallized in DCM:MeOH to yield the product as a dark red solid (3.13 g, 37%). NMR data agreed with the literature values.90 1H NMR (CDCl3, 400 MHz):  = 6.05 (s, 2H), 2.58 (s, 3H), 2.52 (s, 6H), 2.42 (s, 6H).

BODIPY 4.4 (126 mg, 0.481 mmol) was dissolved in ~10 mL of DCM and NIS (97 mg, 0.433 mmol) was added. The mixture was stirred at room temperature for 40 hours with the flask covered with Al foil. 8-(4-(bis(4-methoxyphenyl)amino)phenyl)-5,5-difluoro-1,3,7,9,10-pentamethyl-5H-54,64dipyrrolo[1,2-c:2',1'-f][1,3,2]diazaborinine-2-carbaldehyde (4.7). An oven dried schlenk flask

was charged with DMF (1 mL), and DCE (3 mL). The solution was cooled to 0 oC under nitrogen and POCl3 (1 mL) was added over 5 min. Synthesis of 4.29. To an oven dried schlenk flask, DMF (10 mL) and DCE (30mL) were added under an atmosphere of N2, and the solution was cooled to 0 oC. After stirring the solution at 0 oC for 5 minutes, POCl3 was added, the solution was stirred an additional 5 min at same temperature and then mixture was warmed to room temperature. After 30 min of stirring at RT, a solution of BODIPY (0.26 g, 1 mmol) in 30 mL of DCE was added in one portion. The reaction was stirred for 3 hours at RT. The solution was slowly poured onto 400 mL of saturated NaHCO3 at 0 oC and the resulting mixture was stirred overnight at RT. The organic layer was extracted with DCM, dried over MgSO4, and solvents were removed in vacuo. The residue was purified by column chromatography using DCM as the eluent (Rf = 0.42) yielding the product as an orange-red solid.

Chapter 5: Summary, Conclusions and Future Work
The synthesis of BODIPY-phenylacetylene macrocycles where BODIPY is enclosed within a phenylacetylene macrocycle have been reported herein. Various synthetic strategies were used to make the target molecules; however, a successful synthetic strategy (top-down approach) showed that cyclizing the ring at the bottom was the most effective way to close the ring (chapter 2). Upon synthesis of the BODIPY-phenylacetylene macrocycle 2.31, a control macrocycle 2.34 was made as a benchmark to appreciate energy transfer studies. The control macrocycle (2.34) was not fluorescent in the visible spectrum and upon excitation (287 nm), it emitted at 333 nm with a Stokes shift of 46 nm. However, when the BODIPY-phenylacetylene macrocycle 2.31 is excited at its max (292 nm), the emission is exclusively from the BODIPY core with a Stokes shift of 236 nm. It is also observed that emission always occurs from the BODIPY core, irrespective of the excitation wavelength which suggests that there is an energy transfer from the macrocycle to the BODIPY. The process of energy transfer from the macrocycle to BODIPY is depicted in Figure 5.1.

Figure 5.1. An energy transfer process within the BODIPY-macrocycle

Following the parent BODIPY-phenylacetylene macrocycle 2.31, a more rigid BODIPYphenylacetylene macrocycle 2.39 was made which also showed energy transfer from the macrocycle to the BODIPY core. The BODIPY core in 2.39 is more rigidly orthogonal to the macrocycle as compared to 2.31 which is attributed to the steric effect of the BODIPY methyl
217

groups. It was also observed that both BODIPY-phenylacetylene macrocycles 2.31 and 2.39 exhibit excellent fluorescence quantum yields (0.89% and 0.91% for 2.31 and 2.39, respectively). The BODIPY-phenylacetylene macrocycle design possesses a macrocycle orthogonal to the BODIPY unit and upon excitation, energy transfer is observed from the macrocyclic part of the compound to the BODIPY core. However, the macrocyclic part of the molecule (2.31) absorbs light around 300 nm which is less than ideal for solar applications, because few photons penetrate our atmosphere at this wavelength. For photovoltaic applications, the dye needs to be modified such that the macrocycle absorbs appreciably in the visible (i.e. a red-shift in the absorption profile is required). To this end, modifying the macrocyclic part of the BODIPY-phenylacetylene to redshift the absorption profile was explored and modified BODIPY-phenylacetylene macrocycles were studied as examined in Chapter 3. A bathochromic shift in the absorption envelope was achieved by attaching donor and acceptor groups on the phenylacetylene arms. An attempt to implement a push-pull strategy between donor and acceptor groups was unsuccessful owing to synthetic difficulties; however, the modified macrocycles were successfully made by substituting mono-donor groups (on one side of the macrocycle) (3.29 and 3.37) and bis-donor groups (on both sides of the macrocycle) (3.42). The absorption studies show that the modified BODIPY-phenylacetylene macrocycle with bis-donor group (3.42) displayed the most red-shifted profile when compared with the mono-donor groups (3.29 and 3.37). The absorption envelope of compound 3.42 was extended up to 380 nm which indicates a bathochromic shift of about 50 nm as compared to the parent BODIPYphenylacetylene macrocycle 2.31. The fluorescence studies showed the energy transfer from the macrocycle to the BODIPY upon excitation of the macrocycle. In anticipation of converting these BODIPY -spacers into a D--A dye motif for DSSC applications, a number of unsubstituted-BODIPYs and methylated BODIPYs having different donor groups at the meso-position were synthesized and studied for their physicochemical properties (Chapter 4). The HOMOs lie on the BODIPY core in most of the BODIPYs except two of them (4.22 and 4.26) which have TPA donor groups attached at the meso-position and the HOMOs reside on the TPA unit. However, similar to the rest of the BODIPYs, the LUMOs resides on the meso-position. It was also observed that LUMO energy increases with increased electronic density at the meso-position. Additionally, unsubstituted BODIPYs decompose upon oxidation
218

which results in quasi-reversible oxidation whereas, the methylated BODIPYs are not prone to decomposition and possess a reversible oxidation. Furthermore, formylation and iodination reactions were studied at the 2,6-positions of various BODIPYs. It was observed that both reactions work fairly well regardless of the presence of bulky groups on the BODIPY. However, the formylation reaction can be done more selectively as compared to iodation which is more prone to competition reactions when both the 2- and 6-positions are available on BODIPY. If the iodination reaction is required before the Vilsmeier-Haack formylation reaction, keeping the iodinating agent (NIS) as a limiting reagent can help increase the yield during the iodination reaction. The BODIPY-phenylacetylene macrocycles were made to mimic to energy transfer processes found in Nature. However, the BODIPY-phenylacetylene macrocycles outlined in this studied will not be effective DSSC dyes owing to the fact that the DSSC is supposed to be an economically viable technology and this synthesis is not economical. It could be used for academic purposes but unlikely useful for a commercial project. Based on the current work, the future work is suggested to include, but is not limited to: 1. The optimization of cyclization step using varying amounts of CuI and PdCl2(PPh3)2. This may include a systematic study of how the concentration affects the yield. This might lead to a new catalytic protocol for the cyclization step. Additionally, this kind of systematic study could be done on some other macrocycle which is more easily made. 2. Time resolved (femtosecond) fluorescence studies to determine the rate and efficiency of energy transfer between the BODIPY and the macrocycle (see section 1.7 for details). 3. In order to further appreciate the nature of energy transfer (Forster vs Dexter), a rotaxane based on this design could be readily prepared removing the possibility for Dexter (through bond) energy transfer, leading to a direct comparison with this work. 4. A DSSC dye (D--A) can be made using the BODIPY-macrocycle as a proof of concept. It is hypothesized that the proposed dye design could also have structural benefits amenable for the DSSCs as well. The structural benefits of the target molecules are summarized in Figure 5.2. A simple DA dye motif without macrocycle (Figure 5.2A) is compared to the DA dye motif with the macrocycle (Figure 5.2B). In the absence of macrocycle (Figure 5.2A), the electrolyte can come in contact with the TiO2 semiconductor and a
219

recombination/short circuit event can occur. However, the presence of macrocycle (Figure 5.2B) reduces the possibility of recombination by passivating the surface between the electrolyte and TiO2.

Figure 5.2. Cartoon showing the decrease in the rate recombination and energy transfer from the macrocycle to the spacer
